Intention and the Asymmetry of Trade vs Service Goods
Applies the maxim 'al-umur bi maqasidiha' to the distinction between trade and service goods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi legal maxim 'al-umur bi maqasidiha' to distinguish between trade goods and service goods, identifying when intention alone changes the ruling and when actual sale is required.

Core Idea
Intention transforms trade goods into personal property instantly, but personal goods only become trade goods upon sale.

This lesson unpacks the asymmetry of intention in Hanafi fiqh: why a trader's mere thought of personal use cancels zakat, but a desire to sell a personal item does not trigger zakat until the sale. The governing maxim, 'al-umur bi maqasidiha', yields different outcomes depending on the starting category. Understanding this protects muftis from issuing fatwas that violate the established Hanafi rule on trade goods.

Guided Path
1
State the governing qawida
Identify the maxim 'al-umur bi maqasidiha' and explain its universal scope in Hanafi fiqh, especially in trade, zakat, and endowments.
The maxim is a kubra (major) principle.
2
Apply the qawida to the first furu': trade goods intended for service
Trace how intending service for trade goods immediately removes the goods from trade status, making zakat void.
The text cites the case of a slave girl bought for trade then intended for service.
3
Apply the qawida to the second furu': service goods intended for trade
Note the restriction: intending trade for service goods does not make them trade goods until they are actually sold.
Mere intention is insufficient; a concrete transaction is needed.
4
Identify the istithna' (exception) in tark
In matters of abandoning (tark) an action, intention alone suffices because it is a purely internal act.
This contrasts with the second furu' where intention alone does not convert personal goods.
5
Synthesize the asymmetry
Explain why the two cases differ: trade goods are defined by the intention to profit, so changing that intention immediately reclassifies them; personal goods are defined by use, so a mere profit intention does not override the existing use until sale.
The asymmetry is rooted in the nature of the respective categories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو نوى بمال التجارة: اگر کوئی شخص مال تجارت میں خدمت کی نیت کر لے تو نیت کرتے ہی وہ ضرورت اصلیہ میں داخل ہو جائے گا لہذا اب اس پر مال تجارت کے احکام جاری نہ ہوں گے۔
و من اشترى جارية للتجارة و نو للخدمة بطلت عنها الزكوة (ہندیہ ج ۱ ص: ۷۴ ) او پر جو مسئلہ ذکر ہوا ہے مال تجارت اس کے برعکس ہے لہذا اگر خدمت والے مال میں تجارت کی نیت کرے تو محض نیت سے وہ مال نامی نہ ہوگا۔ ثم ما نواه للخدمة لا يصير للتجارة و ان نوى لها مالم يبعھا، اس کی تفصیل تروک کی بحث میں گذر چکی کہ تروک میں صرف نیت کافی ہے۔
And if one intends for trade goods [to be for service]: If a person makes the intention of service for trade goods, then by merely intending it becomes part of essential needs, so the rulings of trade goods no longer apply to it. And whoever buys a slave girl for trade and intends her for service, the zakat is void from her (Hindiyya, vol. 1, p. 74). The opposite issue was mentioned for trade goods; so if one intends trade for service goods, by mere intention that goods do not become profitable. Then what he intended for service does not become for trade even if he intends for it unless he sells it. The detail of this has passed in the discussion of tark (abandonment) that in tark, mere intention is sufficient.
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage applies the maxim 'al-umur bi maqasidiha' to the distinction between trade goods ('urud al-tijarah) and goods for personal service (mal al-khidmah). The rule: If a person intends service for goods that are trade goods (e.g., a slave girl bought for trade), the goods immediately become personal property and zakat is no longer obligatory upon them. Conversely, if one intends trade for goods that are for personal service, mere intention does not turn them into trade goods; they only become trade goods when actually sold. This asymmetry is because trade goods are defined by the intention to profit, but personal goods require a stronger transition. The author notes that in matters of tark (abandoning something), intention alone suffices, but for changing the category of goods, actual sale is needed. This is a qawida with furuat: the maxim is 'intentions matter', and the furu' are the two cases.

Key Terms
مال التجارة (māl al-tijārah): Goods acquired with the intention of trade, subject to zakat after one lunar year if they reach nisab.
خدمت کی نیت (khidmat kī niyyat): Intention to use a commodity for personal service, e.g., a slave for domestic work, removing it from trade status.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens if someone intends trade for a slave girl currently used for service?
A) She becomes trade goods immediately and zakat is due.
B) She does not become trade goods until she is actually sold.
C) The intention is invalid because she is for service.
D) She becomes trade goods only if the intention is made verbally.

Key Takeaway
Intention alone changes trade goods to personal use instantly, but personal goods become trade goods only upon sale.

Hesitation and Lack of Certainty Undermine Intention
Qawida definition with furu' application

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the principle that hesitation invalidates intention and apply it to a case involving conditional intention in trade goods.

Core Idea
An intention must be decisive; hesitation (taraddud) negates it—conditional intent does not change legal status.

This lesson explores a critical refinement of the maxim 'al-umur bi maqasidiha': intention must not only exist but be firm. A hesitant or conditional intent is legally ineffective. Through a case from Imam Muhammad, we see how this principle affects zakat liability on goods held with a conditional profit motive.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle
State clearly that hesitation (taraddud) and lack of certainty (adam al-jazm) are contrary to a valid intention (niyyah).
This is a qawida restriction on the major maxim.
2
Understand the scenario
Read the fatwa from Imam Muhammad: a person buys a servant for service but conditionally intends to sell if profit arises.
The conditional intent ('if profit, then sell') is not firm.
3
Apply the principle
Because the intention to sell is conditional (hesitant), it does not override the primary intent of service.
Hesitation prevents the intention from being effective.
4
Conclude the ruling
No zakat is due on the servant because it remains a personal asset, not trade goods with a firm trading intention.
This is the mufta bihi ruling.
5
Note the authority
This ruling comes from Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani in al-Multaqat, a source of zahir al-riwayah.
The principle is consistently upheld by later Hanafi scholars.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَضْلٌ: وَمِنَ الْمُنَافِى التردد و عَدَمُ الْجَزْمِ فِي أصْلِهَا وَ فِي الْمُلْتَقَطِ": وَعَن محمد فِيْمَنْ إِشْتَرَىٰ خَادِمًا لِلْخِدْمَةِ، وَهُوَ يَنْوِى إِنْ أَصَابَ رِبْحاً بَاعَهُ لَا زَكَاةَ عَلَيْهِ.
Section: And from what is contrary [to intention] is hesitation and lack of determination in its foundation. And in al-Multaqat: From Muhammad regarding one who bought a servant for service, and he intends that if he profits, he will sell him; there is no zakat upon him.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This brief passage establishes a key principle in the law of intentions: hesitation (taraddud) and lack of certainty (adam al-jazm) are contrary to the nature of a valid intention. For an intention to be legally effective, especially in acts of worship and contractual matters, it must be firm and decisive. The author cites a fatwa from Imam Muhammad: if someone buys a slave for service but intends conditionally (i.e., to sell if profit arises), this conditional intention does not make the slave trade goods subject to zakat because the intention is not firm; the primary purpose remains service. Thus, the rule is that an intention tainted by hesitation does not change the legal status of the item. This refines the application of the maxim 'al-umur bi maqasidiha' by adding the requirement of decisiveness.

Key Terms
تردد (taraddud): Hesitation, wavering between two possibilities; legally negates a firm intention.
عدم الجزم (adam al-jazm): Lack of determination or firm resolve, synonymous with taraddud in effect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on zakat for a person who buys a servant for service but intends to sell it if he makes a profit?
A) Zakat is due because the intention to sell makes it trade goods
B) Zakat is not due because the intention is conditional and not firm
C) Zakat is due after one year regardless of intention
D) Zakat is due only if the servant is actually sold

Key Takeaway
Hesitation (taraddud) negates the legal effect of intention; a conditional intent does not change the primary classification of an asset.

Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims

Effect of Attaching 'In Sha Allah' to Intention vs. Utterances
Clarifies the effect of 'in sha Allah' on intention vs. speech acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi qawida that distinguishes between intention-based and utterance-based acts when 'in sha Allah' is attached, and identify the operative ruling for fatwa.

Core Idea
Intentions are not nullified by 'in sha Allah'; utterances are.

In Hanafi legal methodology, the phrase 'in sha Allah' (if Allah wills) carries different legal effects depending on whether it is attached to an internal intention (like fasting) or a verbal pronouncement (like divorce). This lesson unpacks the governing maxim and its practical application for the mufti, based on the principle of istihsan.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
Recognize that the passage divides acts into 'ma yata'allaqu bil-niyyat' (related to intentions) and 'ma yata'allaqu bil-aqwal' (related to utterances).
Examples given: intention-based: fasting, prayer; utterance-based: divorce, manumission.
2
Apply the qawida
Attaching 'in sha Allah' does not nullify intention-based acts; it does nullify utterance-based acts. Memorize the maxim: 'Ta'liq al-niyyah bil-mashiah la yubtaluha, wa ta'liq al-qawl bil-mashiah yubtaluhu.'
This is the core distinction.
3
Understand the istihsan basis
The passage states that this ruling is based on istihsan (juristic preference). 'In sha Allah' here is not a true exception (istithna) but a request for divine assistance and success, so it does not affect intentions.
Refer to the citation from al-Shami (Ibn Abidin) confirming this as the correct position.
4
Confirm the mufta bihi
The operative ruling for fatwa: intention remains valid; utterance is nullified. This is the established position in the late Hanafi fatwa literature.
No need to consider weaker opinions for this maxim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَرْعُ: عَقْبَ النِّيَّةِ بِالْمَشِيْئَةِ، فَقَدَّمْنَا أَنَّهُ إِنْ كَانَ مِمَّا يَتَعَلَّقُ بِالنِّيَاتِ كَالصَّوْمِ وَ الصَّلَاةِ لَمْ تَبْظُلْ، وَإِنْ كَانَ مِمَّا يَتَعَلَّقُ بِالْأَقْوَالِ كَالطَّلَاقِ وَ الْعِتَاقِ بَطَلَ: ترجمه جزئیہ کسی نے نیت کے بعد انشاء اللہ کہ دیا تو ہم پہلے بیان کر چکے ہیں کہ اگر وہ نیات سے متعلق ہو جسے روزہ اور نماز تو نیت باطل نہیں ہوگی اور اگر وہ اقوال سے متعلق ہو جیسے طلاق اور عتاق تو وہ باطل ہے تشریح ماقبل میں یہ اصول ذکر کیا گیا تھا کہ جملہ عقود و تصرفات جن کا مدار اقوال پر ہے انشاء اللہ ان پر اثر انداز ہوتا ہے اور جن کا مدار اقوال پر نہیں ہے بلکہ نیتوں پر ہے تو وہاں انشاء اللہ کا کچھ اثر نہیں ہوگا۔ ولا تبطل بالمشيئة بل بالرجوع عنها وتحته في الشامية أى استحساناً وهو الصحيح لأنها ليست في معنى حقيقة الاستثناء بل للإستعانة وطلب التوفيق. (شامی ج ۳، ص: ۳۴۵)
Sub-issue: Attaching 'in sha Allah' to the intention. We have previously stated that if it is something related to intentions like fasting and prayer, it does not nullify; and if it is something related to utterances like divorce and freeing slaves, it nullifies. Translation: ... Commentary: In the preceding, it was mentioned that all contracts and transactions based on utterances are affected by 'in sha Allah', but those based on intentions are not. And the niyyah does not become invalid by 'in sha Allah' but by retracting from it. And in al-Shamiya: This is istihsan and correct because it is not in the meaning of true exception but for seeking assistance and success.
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage presents a key distinction in Hanafi fiqh: acts that depend solely on intention (niyyah), such as fasting and prayer, are not invalidated if one says 'in sha Allah' after the intention, because the intention is an internal act not affected by external verbal conditions. However, acts that depend on utterances (aqwal), such as divorce (talaq) and manumission (itaq), are nullified by 'in sha Allah' because those acts are effected by speech, and attaching a condition makes them conditional and thus invalid. The reliance is on istihsan: 'in sha Allah' is not a true istithna (exception) but a request for divine assistance, so it does not affect intention-based acts.

Key Terms
المشيئة (al-mashiah): The phrase 'in sha Allah' (if Allah wills) attached to an act or statement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi school, if someone intends to pray and then says 'in sha Allah', what is the effect?
A) The prayer is invalid because the intention is conditional.
B) The intention remains valid and the prayer is valid.
C) The intention is nullified and must be renewed.
D) The prayer becomes nafl.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, 'in sha Allah' attached to intention does not invalidate the worship, but attached to a verbal pronouncement nullifies it.

Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims

Intentions in Calling Names and Repeating Divorce
Application of maxim to name-calling and divorce repetition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'al-umur bi maqasidiha' to determine the legal effect of ambiguous utterances in divorce and emancipation, distinguishing between diyanatan and qadaan rulings.

Core Idea
Intention determines the religious ruling; court relies on apparent repetition.

This lesson explores two concrete applications of the universal maxim 'al-umur bi maqasidiha' (matters are judged by intentions) from Al-Ashbah wa al-Nazair. We will examine how the ruling changes when a person calls someone by a name that is also a legal term, and when a person repeats a divorce word multiple times. Understanding the interplay between subjective intention and objective evidence is crucial for issuing correct fatwas.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim
Begin by stating the universal maxim 'al-umur bi maqasidiha' and its rank as a major (kubra) qa'ida governing all chapters of fiqh.
Remember that this maxim places emphasis on intention in determining legal consequences.
2
Analyze the first branch: calling by name
Consider the case where a wife is named Taliq or a slave Hurra. The utterance 'Ya Taliq' or 'Ya Hurra' can be a mere call or a legal declaration. Identify the three scenarios: intended divorce/emancipation, intended calling only, or absolute (no specific intention).
The relied-upon opinion for absolute is non-occurrence.
3
Analyze the second branch: repeating divorce
When a person repeats the word 'talaq' multiple times, determine his intention: renewal (istinaf) or emphasis (ta'kid). Recognize the dual ruling: diyanatan (religiously) based on intention, qadaan (judicially) based on apparent repetition.
If absolute (no clear intention), the ruling is the same as for emphasis.
4
Distinguish between diyanatan and qadaan
Understand that the religious ruling (diyanatan) considers the person's conscience, while the court ruling (qadaan) relies on external evidence. This distinction is crucial for a mufti advising on divorce cases.
The court does not accept a claim of emphasis unless supported by evidence or a clarifying phrase.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَرُوْعٌ لَوْ كَانَ إِسْمُهَا طَالِقاً أَوْ حُرَّةً، فَنَادَاهَا إِنْ قَصَدَ الطَّلَاقَ، أَوِ الْعِتْقَ وَقَعَ، أَوِ النِّدَاءَ فَلا، أَوْ أَطْلَقَ فَالْمُعْتَمَدُ عَدَمُهُ. وَلَوْ كَرَّرَ لَفْظَ الطَّلَاقِ، فَإِنْ قَصَدَ الْإِسْتِنَافَ وَقَعَ الْكُلُّ، أَوْ التَّأْكِيدَ فَوَاحِدَةٌ دِيَانَةً، وَالْكُلُّ قَضَاءً، وَكَذَا إِذَا أَطْلَقَ.

ترجمه: جزئیات: اگر اس کا نام طالق یا حرہ ہو اور اس (شوہر) نے اسے آواز لگائی تو اگر طلاق یا عتق کا ارادہ کیا تو وہ واقع ہو جائیں گے اور اگر پکارنے کا ہی ارادہ تھا تو وہ واقع نہیں ہوں گے۔ یا مطلق رکھا (نہ طلاق وعتاق کا ارادہ کیا اور نہ ہی نداء کا) تو معتمد مذہب یہ ہے کہ واقع نہیں ہوں گے۔ اور اگر بار بار لفظ طلاق بولا تو اگر استناف کا ارادہ کیا تو تمام طلاقیں واقع ہو جائیں گی، یا تاکید کا ارادہ کیا تو دیناً ایک واقع ہوگی اور قضاءً تمام واقع ہو جائیں گی۔ اور یہی حکم اس صورت میں ہے جبکہ لفظ طلاق علی الاطلاق بولا ہو۔

تشریح: الأمور بمقاصدها پر چند تفریعات ذکر کی جارہی ہیں۔ اگر کسی کی بیوی کا نام طالق یا باندی کا نام حرہ ہے اور شوہر نے یا طالق کہہ کر یا آقا نے باندی کو یا حرہ کہ کر آواز دی تو کیا اس صورت وہ عورت مطلقہ اور باندی آزاد ہو جائے گی؟ اس مسئلہ کی تین صورتیں ہیں: (۱) اگر شوہر نے طالق سے طلاق اور آقا نے حرہ سے آزادی کا ارادہ کیا ہے تو وہ طلاق وعتاق واقع ہو جائیں گے۔ (۲) اگر صرف پکارنا اور آواز دینا مقصود ہے، طلاق و عتاق نہیں، تو وہ دونوں واقع نہ ہوں گے۔ (۳) اگر مطلق رکھا، نہ طلاق و عتاق کا ارادہ کیا اور نہ پکار کا تو اس صورت میں معتمد قول طلاق وعتاق کے عدم وقوع کا ہے۔

ولو كرر الطلاق: اگر کسی شخص نے لفظ طلاق دیتے ہوئے لفظ طلاق کا تکرار کیا، تو اگر اس سے مقصود استئناف ہے تو تینوں واقع ہو جائیں گی، اور اگر مقصود تاکید ہے تو دیناً تو ایک ہی طلاق واقع ہوگی لیکن قضاءً تین طلاق کے حکم میں ہوں گے؟ (اصل عبارت میں: واالكل قضاءً یعنی قضاءً سب واقع ہوں گے) اور اگر مطلق بولا تو وہی حکم ہے کہ دیناً نیت پر اعتبار کیا جائے گا۔
Branches: If her name is Taliq or Hurra, and he calls her, if he intended divorce or emancipation, it takes effect; if he intended the call, it does not; if he was absolute (no intent), the relied-upon is that it does not take effect. If he repeated the word divorce, if he intended renewal, all take effect; if he intended emphasis, then one [divorce] takes effect religiously (diyanatan), but all take effect judicially (qadaan). Similarly if he was absolute. Translation: Details: If a woman's name is Taliq or a slave girl's name is Hurra, and the husband called her, if he intended divorce or freedom, they occur; if he intended just calling, they do not. If he was absolute (no intent of divorce/call), the relied-upon opinion is non-occurrence. If he repeated the word of divorce: if he intended renewal, all occur; if he intended emphasis, then religiously one occurs but judicially all occur; same if he was absolute. Explanation: Several branches are being mentioned on the maxim 'matters are judged by intentions'. If a wife's name is Taliq or a slave's name is Hurra, and the husband called her 'ya Taliq' or the master called her 'ya Hurra', will the wife be divorced and the slave freed? There are three possibilities: (1) If the husband intended divorce and the master intended freedom, they occur. (2) If they intended only calling, they do not occur. (3) If they had no specific intention, the relied-upon opinion is non-occurrence. As for repeating the word of divorce: if a person uttered 'talaq' repeatedly, if he intended renewal, all divorces occur; if he intended emphasis, then religiously only one divorce occurs but judicially all occur. If he was absolute, the ruling is similar: diyanatan his intention is considered.
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage presents two branches (furu') derived from the universal maxim 'al-umur bi maqasidiha' (matters are judged by intentions). First branch: If a husband calls his wife whose name is 'Taliq' (which is also the word for divorce) and says 'Ya Taliq', does a divorce occur? Similarly, if a master calls his slave girl named 'Hurra' (which is the word for free) saying 'Ya Hurra', does she become free? The ruling depends on intention. If he intended divorce/emancipation, it takes effect. If he intended only to call by name, it does not. If he had no specific intention (absolute), the relied-upon Hanafi position is that it does not take effect, because the literal usage here is for calling, and the word itself is ambiguous. Second branch: If a person repeats the word 'talaq' multiple times in one instance, does one or multiple divorces occur? The ruling depends on intention. If he intended renewal (istinaf), meaning each utterance is a fresh divorce, then all occur. If he intended emphasis (ta'kid), meaning he meant to emphasize a single divorce, then religiously (diyanatan, i.e., in his conscience) only one divorce occurs, but judicially (qadaan, i.e., in court) all occur because the court does not accept his claim of emphasis unless there is a clarifying phrase. If he was absolute (no intention), the same as emphasis applies: diyanatan one, qadaan all. This demonstrates the interplay between subjective intention and objective evidence in Hanafi law.

Key Terms
استئناف (istinaf): Renewal; in the context of repeating divorce, it means each utterance is a fresh start of divorce, not a repetition of the same.
تأكيد (ta'kid): Emphasis; the intention to confirm a prior divorce by repeating the word for reinforcement.
دِيَانَةً (diyanatan): Religiously, according to the individual's conscience and what they believe to be true before God.
قَضَاءً (qadaan): Judicially, according to the court's ruling based on apparent evidence.
مطلق (mutlaq): Absolute; without specification of intention or qualification.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Substitution in Intention: Tayammum, Zakat, and Hajj
Definition of substitution in intention with three case examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between acts where intention can be delegated (tayammum, zakat) and where it must be personal (hajj badal), and apply the qawida to similar fatwa scenarios.

Core Idea
Intention is personal, but in tayammum and zakat the principal's intention suffices; in hajj badal the agent's own intention is required.

This passage from Al-Ashbah wa al-Nazair explores the subtle rule of niyabah fi an-niyyah—whether one person's intention can substitute for another's in acts of worship. The author presents three distinct cases from Hanafi fiqh, showing where the principal's intention is considered (tayammum, zakat) and where it is not (hajj). Understanding this distinction is crucial for muftis when issuing fatwas on delegation in worship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the qawida
The passage defines the concept of niyabah fi an-niyyah (substitution in intention) and provides examples where it applies or not. Identify the general maxim: intention is normally personal, but exceptions exist.
The qawida is a dabt (sub-maxim) restricting the principle that intention is a personal obligation.
2
Analyze the tayammum case
When a sick person's tayammum is performed by a helper, the intention belongs to the sick person (principal), not the helper. This is because the act is considered the sick person's own act of worship.
The helper is merely an instrument; the obligation rests on the sick person.
3
Analyze the zakat case
If a principal intends to pay zakat and appoints an agent to distribute, the agent's distribution is valid even without separate intention. The principal's intention is considered.
Here, the agent is a delegate for the physical act, but intention already exists from the principal.
4
Analyze the hajj case
In hajj on behalf of another (hajj badal), the agent's own intention is required. The text explicitly says this is not substitution in intention because the agent performs the actions.
This shows that intention cannot be delegated when the agent personally performs the core rituals.
5
Derive the underlying principle
The distinction hinges on whether the act is purely physical (like distribution of zakat or performing tayammum on someone) or requires personal performance (like hajj rituals). In the former, the principal's intention suffices; in the latter, the agent must intend.
This principle helps muftis analyze new cases of delegation in worship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تكميل فى النيابة في النية. قَالَ فِي التَّيَمُّمِ: مَرِيضٌ يَتَيَمَّمُ غَيْرُهُ، فَالنِّيَّةُ عَلَى الْمَرِيضِ دُونَ الْمُيَمِّمِ. وَفِي الزَّكَاةِ: الْمُعْتَبَرُ نِيَّةُ الْمُوَكِّلِ، فَلَوْ نَوَاهَا وَدَفَعَ الْوَكِيلُ بِلَا نِيَّةٍ أَجْزَأَهُ. وَفِي الْحَجِّ عَنِ الْغَيْرِ: الِاعْتِبَارُ لِنِيَّةِ الْمَأْمُورِ، وَلَيْسَ هُوَ مِنْ بَابِ النِّيَابَةِ فِي النِّيَّةِ، لِأَنَّ الْأَفْعَالَ إِنَّمَا صَدَرَتْ مِنَ الْمَأْمُورِ.
A completion concerning substitution in intention. He said in tayammum: A sick person whose tayammum is performed by another, then the intention is upon the sick person, not the one who performs tayammum. And in zakat: The intention of the principal (muwakkil) is considered; if he intended it and the agent paid without intention, it suffices. And in hajj on behalf of another: Consideration is given to the intention of the commissioned agent (ma'mur), and this is not from the chapter of substitution in intention because the actions are only performed by the agent.
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage is a supplement (takmilah) discussing the concept of niyabah (substitution) in intention for specific acts. For tayammum, if a sick person cannot perform it himself and someone else performs tayammum on him, the intention is that of the sick person, not the helper. For zakat, if the principal intends to give zakat and appoints an agent to distribute it, the agent's distribution without separate intention is valid because the principal's intention suffices. For hajj on behalf of another (hajj badal), the intention of the agent (the one performing the hajj) is considered, not the principal's. This is because the agent performs the actions, so the intention belongs to him. Hence, this is not true substitution in intention; rather, the agent's own intention is required. The commentator notes that the author intended to give examples where intention can be delegated but did not do so explicitly.

Key Terms
النيابة في النية (an-niyabah fi an-niyyah): Substitution in intention; whether one's intention can stand for another's.
موكل (muwakkil): The principal who appoints an agent (wakil).
مأمور (ma'mur): The commissioned agent; in hajj badal, the one who performs hajj on behalf of another.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, in hajj performed on behalf of another, whose intention is considered?
A) The principal's intention
B) The agent's intention
C) Both
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
Substitution in intention is only valid when the act is attributed to the principal (as in tayammum and zakat); when the agent personally performs the acts (as in hajj badal), the agent's own intention is required.

Applying the Maxim 'Al-Umuru Bi-Maqasidiha' Across Fiqh Chapters
List of furuat (subsidiary rulings) for the maxim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'al-umuru bi-maqasidiha' to determine the ruling of an act based on the agent's intention in diverse fiqh scenarios.

Core Idea
The same verbal act can be halal or haram depending solely on what the speaker intends.

This lesson presents five real-world applications of the grand maxim 'al-umuru bi-maqasidiha'. As a mufti, you must learn to evaluate not just the outward act but the intention behind it. Each case shows how a single utterance receives a different legal ruling based on what the person meant.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the intention
For each case, first state the physical action (e.g., reciting Quran, saying 'alhamdulillah'). Then determine the possible intentions that the actor might have had.
Intention is internal; we deduce it from context if not stated.
2
Apply the maxim to each intention
Using 'al-umuru bi-maqasidiha', derive the ruling for each possible intention. The same act may receive different rulings based on intent.
3
Locate the furu' in its fiqh chapter
Note that the maxim operates across ibadat, mu'amalat, and slaughters. Identify which chapter each case belongs to.
This shows the maxim is universal.
4
Extract the principle for fatwa
Formulate a general principle: 'When a single utterance can serve multiple purposes, its fiqh ruling depends on the intention, not the literal wording.'
This principle guides muftis in analogous cases.

ذبح فعطس : بوقت ذبح چھینک آئی اور ذابح نے الحمد للہ کہا تو اگر تسمیہ علی المد بوح کی نیت سے کہا ہے تو بیجھ مفسد صلوٰۃ ہے۔
Like recitation becomes forbidden, and if the purpose is to recite as remembrance there is no sin. And among them: if a junub recites: a person recites Quran in state of janaba, if his intent is (for) a sign and not (recitation), and if he recites with intention of tilawat, then it is makruh. If he recites al-Fatiha: if a person recites al-Fatiha in funeral prayer with the intention of supplication and praise. The khatib sneezes: the khatib says alhamdulillah during the sermon, if his intent is to deliver the sermon, then alhamdulillah suffices for the sermon, and if his intent is to respond to the sneeze, then this alhamdulillah does not suffice. Also note: this mas'alah is according to those who consider saying alhamdulillah in the sermon sufficient. It becomes lawful, and if he said it in response to a sneeze, the slaughtered animal does not become lawful. Mention of the musalli (reciting) an ayah: a person is praying, someone asked permission to enter the house, so he said 'on the way with peace, amin'? Then his prayer becomes invalid because the general rule is that during prayer, reciting an ayah with the intention of answering invalidates prayer. Slaughtering and sneezing: at the time of slaughtering a sneeze came and the slaughterer said alhamdulillah; if he said it with the intention of tasmiyah (saying Bismillah) upon the slaughter, then the meat becomes lawful, and if he said it in response to the sneeze, the slaughtered animal is not lawful.

Commentary
This passage presents several subsidiary rulings (furuat) that illustrate the grand maxim 'al-umuru bi-maqasidiha' (matters are determined by their intentions). The author lists cases from different chapters of fiqh: a person in a state of janabah reciting Quran is only sinful if he intends tilawah; reciting al-Fatiha in funeral prayer is permissible if intended as supplication; a khatib's utterance of 'alhamdulillah' during the sermon counts as part of the sermon only if he intends so; responding to a question during prayer by reciting an ayah with the intention of answering invalidates the prayer; and mentioning Allah at the time of slaughter renders the meat lawful only if intended as tasmiyah. In each case, the ruling hinges on the intention.

Key Terms
نیة (niyyah): The intention that distinguishes acts and determines their legal ruling

Key Takeaway

Applying the Grand Maxim to Arabic Grammar: The Definition of Kalam
Concluding note on the maxim's vast scope and its application in Arabic linguistics

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim al-umuru bi-maqasidiha to the definition of kalam (speech) in Arabic linguistics and explain why unintentional utterances are not considered kalam.

Core Idea
The grand maxim al-umuru bi-maqasidiha determines even the definition of speech: kalam requires intention.

In this concluding section, the author emphasizes the all-encompassing nature of the grand maxim 'al-umuru bi-maqasidiha'. He then demonstrates its application beyond fiqh: in Arabic grammar, the very definition of 'kalam' (speech) depends on intention. The majority of grammarians, led by Sibawayhi, require intention for an utterance to be called kalam. This shows that the principle of intentionality is fundamental in both law and language.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the vast scope
The author states that the maxim encompasses numerous rules and countless subsidiary cases. Only the most important have been mentioned; students can derive more.
This highlights the maxim's centrality.
2
Identify the extension to Arabic
The maxim also applies in Arabic language sciences. The first application is in defining speech (kalam).
The universality of the maxim is being asserted.
3
Analyze the definition of kalam
Sibawayhi and the majority condition intention (qasd). Unintentional utterances (sleep, forgetfulness, animal imitation) are not kalam.
Intention is the operative element.
4
Note the dissenting view
Abu Hayyan and a minority do not require intention, considering all utterances kalam.
This is a legitimate linguistic difference.
5
Understand the implication
In both fiqh and language, intention determines classification. A mufti relies on this for rulings on sleep talk or forgetful oaths.
Practical relevance for fatwa.

Applying the Maxim 'Al-Umur bi-Maqasidiha' in Arabic Linguistics and Fiqh
Application of the grand maxim in linguistics and fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'al-umur bi-maqasidiha' to cases involving the definition of speech and the obligation of sajdah, and distinguish between different rulings based on the reciter's capacity.

Core Idea
Intention defines speech and determines obligation; a sleeping reciter still triggers sajdah.

This concluding passage from al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir demonstrates the wide-ranging applicability of the grand maxim 'al-umur bi-maqasidiha'. The author applies it first to the linguistic definition of speech, then to two fiqh cases: an oath not to speak and hearing a verse of sajdah. By studying these applications, you will see how the same maxim governs seemingly unrelated areas of Shari'ah.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its rank
Begin by stating the maxim 'al-umur bi-maqasidiha' (matters are determined by intentions). Recognize it as one of the five major (kubra) qawa'id in the Hanafi school, applicable across all chapters of fiqh.
This maxim is also known as 'al-niyyat' or 'al-qasd' in other formulations.
2
Identify the first application: definition of kalam
The author applies the maxim to the linguistic definition of speech (kalam). According to Sibawayh and the majority, intention (qasd) is a condition for something to be considered true speech. Utterances from a sleeping person, a negligent person, or a trained animal lack this intention and are not classified as kalam.
The term 'kalam' here carries both linguistic and legal significance, especially in oaths.
3
Derive the fiqh branch from the linguistic definition: oath not to speak
Building on that definition, the author presents a case: if a person swears not to speak to someone and then speaks to him while he is asleep (and the sleeper hears), does he break the oath? According to the relied-upon position (mukhtar) of our mashayikh, the oath is not broken unless the sleeper is awakened. This is because the sleeping person lacks attentive intention, so the speech is not considered true speech with respect to the oath.
This shows how a linguistic rule directly impacts a legal ruling (thamarah al-khilaf).
4
Identify the second application: hearing sajdah verse from various reciters
The maxim is also applied to the obligation of sajdah upon hearing a verse of sajdah. The ruling depends on the reciter's legal capacity (ahliyyah) and intention. Hearing from an animal does not obligate sajdah because the animal lacks intention. Hearing from a sleeping or drunk person does obligate sajdah because they are qualified reciters (ahl al-qira'ah). Hearing from an insane person does not. The exception is hearing from a junub or menstruating woman, which obligates sajdah despite their state, because they are still ‘reciters’ and the verse is intended as Qur'an.
The istithna' of junub/haid reinforces the maxim: the intention (reciting Qur'an) outweighs the impurity.
5
Examine the istithna' and the reasoning
The author notes that hearing from a junub or menstruating woman obligates sajdah, unlike hearing from an animal. This is because a junub or ha'id is still a ‘reciter’ (ahl al-qira'ah) and their recitation is valid as Qur'an, even if they cannot pray. The maxim ‘al-umur bi-maqasidiha’ governs: the intention to recite Qur'an is present, so the hearer must perform sajdah. This exception confirms the general rule rather than breaking it.
This exception is an important nuance: the maxim applies to the reciter's 'ahl al-qira'ah' capacity.

الأمُوْرِ بِمَقَاصِدِهَا فِي عِلْمِ الْعَرَبِيَّةِ أَيْضًا، فَأَوَّلُ مَا اعْتَبَرُوْا ذَلِكَ في الكلام، فَقَالَ سِيْبْوَيْهِ وَالْجَمْهُورُ: بِاشْتِرَاطِ القَصْدِ فِيْهِ، فَلَا يُسَمًّى كَلَاماً مَا نَطَقَ بِهِ النَّائِمُ وَالسَّاهِي وَ مَاتَحْكِيْهِ الْحَيَوَانَاتُ الْمُعَلَّمَةُ، وَخَالَفَ بَعْضُهُمْ فَلَمْ يَشْتَرِطُهُ وَسَمَّى كُلَّ ذَلِكَ كَلَاماً، وَ اخْتَارَهُ أَبُو حَيَّانَ. ترجمه ناتمر الامور بمقاصدها" کا قاعدہ علم عربی (لغت) میں بھی چلتا ہے چنانچہ سب سے پہلے کلام میں اس کا اعتبار کیا پس سیبویہ اور جمہور کلام میں قصد کے شرط ہونے کے قائل ہوئے پیس ٹائم اور ساہی اور سدھائے ہوئے جانور Warning: The qa'ida 'al-umur bi-maqasidiha' includes several rules, as has become clear to you. We have covered its most important issues; otherwise, its issues cannot be counted and its branches cannot be exhausted. [Urdu translation and commentary.] Conclusion: The qa'ida 'al-umur bi-maqasidiha' also applies in Arabic linguistics. First, they considered it in speech: Sibawayh and the majority stipulate intention (qasd) in it, so what a sleeping person, a negligent person, and what trained animals utter is not called speech. Some disagreed and did not stipulate it, calling all of that speech; Abu Hayyan chose this. [Urdu translation and commentary.] And based on that, from fiqh: if someone swore not to speak to him, then he spoke to him while he was asleep such that he (the sleeper) heard, then he (the oath-taker) becomes a breaker of the oath. In some riwayat of al-Mabsut, it is conditioned that he wakes him up, and our mashayikh follow that, because if he does not wake up, it is like calling him from afar while he does not hear his voice, as in al-Hidayah. The outcome is that there is difference in the correct position, as we explained in the sharh. I have not yet seen the ruling when he speaks to one who is unconscious, insane, or drunk. And if one hears a verse of sajdah from an animal, they explicitly state that it is not obligatory according to the chosen opinion because the reciter lacks qualification; unlike if he hears it from a junub or a menstruating woman. Hearing from an insane person does not make it obligatory; hearing from a sleeping person makes it obligatory according to the chosen opinion; similarly, it becomes obligatory by hearing it from a drunk person. [Urdu translation and commentary.]
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage is the conclusion (khatima) of the discussion on the grand qawa'id, focusing on the maxim 'al-umur bi-maqasidiha' (matters are determined by intentions). The author first notes the vastness of the maxim's applications, then provides two specific applications: one in the field of Arabic linguistics and one in fiqh rulings. In Arabic linguistics, the definition of 'kalam' (speech) is tied to intention; Sibawayh and the majority require qasd (intention) for something to be considered speech, excluding sleep-talk, negligent utterances, and animal sounds. Abu Hayyan and others disagreed, considering all utterances as speech. In fiqh, the rule is applied to two issues: (1) an oath not to speak to someone – if one speaks to a sleeping person who hears, according to the relied-upon position of our mashayikh (based on a riwaya in al-Mabsut), the oath is broken only if the sleeper is awakened; otherwise, it is like calling from a distance where the call is not heard. (2) Hearing a verse of sajdah – if heard from an animal, sajdah is not wajib because the reciter lacks legal capacity; from a junub or menstruating woman, it is wajib (since they are qualified reciters); from an insane person, not wajib; from a sleeping or drunk person, it is wajib according to the chosen opinion.

Key Terms
قاعدة الأمور بمقاصدها (al-qa'ida: al-umur bi-maqasidiha): The legal maxim that actions and utterances are judged by the intention behind them.
كلام (kalam): Speech that requires intention (qasd) to be considered legally significant; without intention it is not kalam.
سجدة (sajdah): Prostration required upon hearing a specific Qur'anic verse (ayat as-sajdah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon opinion in this passage, if a person hears a verse of sajdah from a sleeping person, is sajdah wajib upon the hearer?
A) Yes, it is wajib because the sleeper's recitation is still considered kalam.
B) No, it is not wajib because the sleeper lacks intention.
C) It is wajib only if the sleeper is awakened.
D) It is not wajib because the sleeper is not a qualified reciter (ahl al-qira'ah).

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'al-umur bi-maqasidiha' requires intention for speech to be legally relevant, and the obligation of sajdah depends on the reciter's capacity, not merely the sound.

Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims
intermediate
10 min
Applying Intentions to Poetry, Calling, and Transferred Nouns
Expanding the maxim of intentions to poetry, indefinite caller, and transferred proper nouns

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'al-umur bi maqasidiha' to determine the legal/linguistic status of metered speech, indefinite callers, and transferred proper nouns.

Core Idea
Intent determines the ruling: metered speech without poetic intent is not poetry.

The major Hanafi maxim 'al-umur bi maqasidiha' (matters are judged by intentions) is not confined to acts of worship alone. In this passage, the author expands its application to three linguistic and legal domains: poetry (al-‘urud), the indefinite caller in Arabic grammar (al-munadi al-nakirah), and proper nouns transferred from adjectives (al-‘ilm al-manqul). In each case, the speaker's intention decides the legal and grammatical classification. Understanding this extension is crucial for muftis when evaluating statements in rulings, particularly in determining whether metered speech qualifies as poetry affecting prayer validity.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its scope
Restate 'al-umur bi maqasidiha' and recognize that it governs not only fiqh acts but also linguistic and grammatical judgments.
The maxim is a kubra (major) and universal (kulliya).
2
Identify the three areas of application
List the three domains: (1) metered speech (poetry), (2) indefinite caller, (3) transferred proper nouns.
Each area has a default ruling that changes based on intent.
3
For each area, locate the role of intent
For poetry: mere meter is insufficient; the speaker must intend to compose poetry. For caller: if a specific person is intended, the noun becomes definite and takes dammah; if not, it remains indefinite and takes nasb. For transferred nouns: if the original adjectival meaning is intended, alif-lam may be entered; otherwise not.
Intent is the decisive factor in each case.
4
Apply the exception: Quran and Hadith
Metered speech in the Quran and authentic Hadith is excluded from poetry because the intent is guidance, not poetic composition. Cite the proof: Quran 36:69.
This exception is itself derived from the same maxim.
5
Derive practical rulings for each furu’
For metered speech in prayer: if it is Quran or Hadith, it does not invalidate. For indefinite caller: case endings differ based on intention. For transferred nouns: alif-lam may or may not be attached depending on intended meaning.
These rulings are directly relevant to legal interpretation and fatwa.

وَتَجْرِى هَذِهِ الْقَاعِدَةُ فِي الْعُرُوضِ أَيْضًا، فَإِنَّ الشَّعْرَ عِنْدَ أَهْلِهِ كَلَامٌ مَوْزُونٌ مَقْصُودٌ بِهِ ذلِكَ، أَمَّا مَا يَقَعُ مَوْزُونًا إِتِّفَاقَاً، لَا عَنْ قَصْدِ مِنَ الْمُتَكَلِّمِ، فَإِنَّهُ لَا يُسَمَّى شِعْراً. وَعَلَى ذلِكَ خَرَجَ مَا وَقَعَ فِي كَلَامِ اللَّهِ تَعَالَىٰ كَقَوْلِهِ تَعَالَى: لَنْ تَنَالُوا الْبِرَّ حَتَّى تُنْفِقُوا مِمَّا تُحِبُّونَ، أَوْ فِي كَلَامِ رَسُوْلِهِ صلى الله عليه وسلم كَقَوْلِهِ: هَلْ أَنْتَ إِلَّا إِصْبَعٌ دَمِيْتَ * وَفِي سَبِيْلِ اللَّهِ مَا لَقِيْتَ.

منقول ہے تو الف لام داخل ہو سکتا ہے اور اگر اس سے متعین شخص کی ذات کو پکار نا مقصود ہے تو اس پر الف لام داخل نہ ہو گا کیونکہ وہ پہلے ہی سے معرفہ ہے اور اگر وصفی معنی کا اظہار مقصود ہے تو اس کا حکم سب سے پہلے ذکر کر دیا گیا ہے کہ الف لام داخل ہو سکتا ہے۔ و تجرى هذه القاعدة في العروض : قرآن و حدیث میں جو موزوں کلمات ہیں وہ شعر میں داخل نہیں چونکہ شعر ایسے موزوں کلام کا نام ہے جس کو قاتل کا مقصد مجمع بنانا ہے لہذا اللہ تعالیٰ کے کلام میں جو موزوں آیتیں ہیں ان کو شعر نہیں کہا جائے گا مثلاً آیت کريمة لن تنالوا البر حتى تنفقوا مما تحبون اس طرح آپ صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم کے تمام کلام میں بھی جو موزوں کلام ہے وہ بھی شعر نہیں مثلا جب آپ کی انگلی مبارکہ پر زخم لگا تو آپ کی زبان سے برجستہ نکلا هل ان الا اصبع دميت و في سبيل الله مالقيت یہ جملہ بظاہر شعر ہے لیکن آپ نے شعر کے مقصد سے نہیں پڑھا بلکہ اتفاقا یہ کلام صادر ہوا کیونکہ اللہ تعالیٰ نے فرمایا ہے و ما علمنه الشعر و ما ينبغى له ـ . و علی ذلک خرج الخ: نیز کلام اللہ میں اور کلام رسول میں لائن قصد سے مراد شعر و شاعری ہے تا کہ اعتراض واقع نہ ہو کہ اللہ اور اس کے رسول سے بدون قصد کلام صادر ہوا ہے کیونکہ اللہ اور اس کے رسول کا مقصد اپنی شایان شان کلام سے لوگوں کو فائدہ پہنچانا ہے۔ و الله اعلم و علمه اتم
This rule also applies to poetry: poetry, according to its people, is metered speech intended as such. As for what occurs metered accidentally, not by the speaker's intent, it is not called poetry. On this basis, what occurs in the speech of Allah Most High, like His saying: 'You will never attain righteousness until you spend from what you love,' or in the speech of His Messenger, like his saying: 'You are nothing but a finger that bled, and in the way of Allah you have met [what you met].' — Translation: And from this category is the indefinite caller: if the intent is to call a specific person, then it becomes definite and must be in the nominative case; otherwise it remains indefinite and is given the accusative case. And from this category is the ruling of a noun transferred from an adjective: if the intention is to manifest that adjective from which it is transferred, then alif-lam is entered upon it; otherwise not. And its particulars are many. And this rule applies to poetry as well: according to poets, poetry is metered speech intended as poetry. And speech that becomes metered accidentally without the speaker's intent is not called poetry. For this reason, what is metered in the speech of Allah Most High, like 'You will never attain righteousness…', and in the speech of the Messenger of Allah, like 'You are nothing but a finger…' is excluded from being poetry. Explanation: The indefinite caller is given the case ending according to the rule of caller; if a specific person is intended, it is definite and takes dammah; if not, it takes nasb. And from that is the transferred proper noun: if a noun is transferred from an adjective, when using it, if the literal adjectival meaning is intended, alif-lam can be entered; if used as a proper noun without the adjective meaning, then if it is a simple (non-compound) proper noun, alif-lam does not enter; if it is a transferred proper noun, alif-lam may enter. And if calling a specific person is intended, alif-lam does not enter because it is already definite; but if the adjectival meaning is intended, the earlier ruling applies. And this rule applies to poetry: the metered words in the Quran and Hadith are not included in poetry because poetry is metered speech intended by the speaker to be poetry. Therefore, metered verses in the Quran are not called poetry, e.g., the noble verse 'You will never attain righteousness…' Similarly, the metered speech of the Prophet is not poetry, e.g., when his blessed finger was injured, he spontaneously said: 'You are nothing but a finger that bled, and in the way of Allah you have met.' This sentence is apparently poetry, but he did not recite it with the intent of poetry; it occurred accidentally, because Allah said: 'And We did not teach him poetry, nor is it befitting for him.' And on that basis: also in the speech of Allah and His Messenger, the mention of intent refers to poetry and versification, so that no objection arises that speech without intent occurred from Allah and His Messenger, because Allah and His Messenger intend to benefit people with speech befitting their status. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the major legal maxim 'al-umur bi maqasidiha' (matters are judged by intentions). The author applies this maxim to three distinct areas: poetry (al-‘urud), indefinite caller (al-munadi al-nakirah), and transferred proper nouns (al-‘ilm al-manqul). In each case, intention is the decisive factor. For poetry, mere metrical form is insufficient; the speaker must intend to compose poetry. Hence, metered verses from the Quran and Hadith are not classified as poetry because they were not spoken with poetic intent. For the indefinite caller in Arabic grammar, the case ending (dammah for nominative or fatha for accusative) depends on whether the speaker intends a specific person or a generic one. For transferred proper nouns (e.g., 'afdal' used as a name), the permissibility of entering alif-lam depends on whether the intended meaning is the original adjectival sense or the proper noun sense. The author emphasizes that these applications derive from the same root principle: intention determines legal and linguistic categorization.

Key Terms
العروض (al-'urud): The field of poetic meter; here referring to metered speech in general
المنادى النكرة (al-munadi al-nakirah): An indefinite noun used in calling (vocative), e.g., 'yā rajul'
العلم المنقول (al-'ilm al-manqul): A proper noun originally derived from an adjective, like 'Afḍal'

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the maxim 'al-umur bi maqasidiha', why is the verse 'Lan tanalū al-birra...' not considered poetry?
A) Because it lacks meter
B) Because it was not spoken with the intent to compose poetry
C) Because it is not in Arabic
D) Because it was revealed after the Prophet's migration

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'al-umur bi maqasidiha' governs even linguistic classifications: intent determines whether metered speech is poetry, how an indefinite caller is inflected, and when a transferred noun takes alif-lam.

Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims

Third Maxim: Certainty Not Removed by Doubt
Introduction and evidence for the third major qa'idah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to STATE the third major qa'idah, IDENTIFY its hadith evidence, and APPLY the definitions of yaqeen, shakk, zann, and ghalib al-zann to fiqh cases.

Core Idea
Certainty is never undone by mere doubt; only sensory evidence breaks it.

This lesson introduces one of the five universal maxims of Hanafi fiqh: certainty is not removed by doubt. This principle guides countless rulings in purity, prayer, and contracts. The author presents the hadith evidence and clarifies key terminology that every mufti must master.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its Arabic text
Memorize the Arabic wording of the third major qa'idah: 'اليقين لا يزول بالشك'. This is a foundational principle.
Repeat it several times until it becomes automatic.
2
Locate the hadith evidence
Identify the hadith cited by the author from Sahih Muslim. Note the discrepancy: the author's wording differs slightly from Muslim's exact wording, but the meaning is the same.
The hadith is about not leaving the mosque due to doubt about passing wind.
3
Analyze the hadith's legal implication
The hadith teaches that mere doubt does not break wudu; only sensory evidence (sound or smell) does. This directly supports the maxim.
Understand the condition: certainty of purity remains until a sensory proof of impurity arises.
4
Define the key terms
Understand yaqeen (heart's satisfaction), shakk (equal hesitation), zann (inclination to one side without discarding the other), and ghalib al-zann (preponderant opinion). These distinctions are crucial for applying the maxim.
In Hanafi usul, these categories determine legal weight.
5
Address the philosophical objection
The author answers how doubt can 'remove' certainty when they cannot coexist: doubt does not remove an established certainty; it simply cannot override it.
The doubt is ineffective; the ruling remains on the original certainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بِسْمِ اللهِ الرَّحمٰنِ الرَّحِيمِ ألْقَاعِدَةُ الثَّالِثَةُ الْيَقِينُ لَا يَزُولُ بِالشَّكِ وَدَلِيْلُهَا مَارَوَاهُ مُسْلِمٌ عَنْ أَبِي هُرَيْرَةَ مَرْفُوْعاً إِذَا وَجَدَ أَحَدُكُمْ فِي بَطْبِهِ شَيْئاً فَاشْكُل عَلَيْهِ اخْرَجَ مِنْهُ شَيءٌ أَمْ لَا فَلَا يَخْرُ جَنَّ مِنَ الْمَسْجِدِ حَتَّى يَسْمَعُ صَوْتاً أوْ يَجِد ريحاً. ترجمه: تیسرا قاعدہ یقین شک سے زائل نہیں ہوتا اور اس کی دلیل وہ روایت ہے جس کو امام مسلم نے حضرت ابو ہریرہ سے مرفوعاً روایت کیا ہے کہ جب تم میں سے کوئی اپنے پیٹ میں کچھ محسوس کرے پھر اسے شبہ ہو کہ اس سے کچھ نکلا ہے یا نہیں تو وہ بالکل مسجد سے نہ نکلے یہاں تک کہ کوئی آواز سن لے یا کچھ بد بو محسوس کرلے۔ تشریح: مصنف تیسرا قاعدہ بیان فرمارہے ہیں کہ یقین شک کی بناء پر زائل نہیں ہوگا اوراس کا ماخذ و اصل و حدیث شریف ہے جو حیحین میں ہے صاحب کتاب نے مسلم کے حوالہ سے جو حدیث کے الفاظ نقل کئے ہیں وہ مسلم میں نہیں ہیں بلکہ مسلم کے الفاظ یہ ہیں: عن عبدالله بن زيد قال شكى إلى النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم لا رجل يخيل إليه انه يجد الشي في الصلاة قال لا ينصرف حتى يسمع صوتاً او يجد ريحاً - سوال: شک یقین کے ساتھ جمع نہیں ہو سکتا تو جس کا وجود نہیں اس کے مرتفع ہونے کا کیا مطلب ہے۔ جواب: اس طرح دیا جا سکتا ہے کہ اصل متیقن کو طاری ہونے والا شیک زائل نہیں کر سکتا۔ کسی شیء کی حقیقت پر اطمینان قلب کو یقین کہتے ہیں کہا جاتا ہے ييـقــن الـمـاء في الحوض “ پراس وقت استعمال ہوتا ہے جب پانی حوض میں ٹھہر جاتا ہے اور لغت میں شک مطلق تردد کو کہتے ہیں۔ اصطلاح اصول میں کسی شئے دونوں طرفوں کا برابر ہونا اور دو چیزوں کے بیچ اس طرح ٹھہر نا کہ دل ان دونوں میں سے کسی کی طرف مائل نہ ہو اس کو شک کہتے ہیں اور اگر دونوں طرفوں میں سے ایک کی طرف دل مائل ہو اور دوسری کو نہ ہٹائے تو اس کو ظن کہتے ہیں اور اگر دوسری جانب کو بھی ایک طرف کر دے تو اس کو غالب الظن کہتے ہیں جو
In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. The third principle: Certainty is not removed by doubt. And its evidence is what Muslim narrated from Abu Hurairah, raised to the Prophet: 'If one of you feels something in his belly, then it becomes doubtful to him whether something came out of it or not, then let him not leave the mosque until he hears a sound or finds an odor.' Translation: The third principle: Certainty is not removed by doubt. And its evidence is the narration that Imam Muslim narrated from Abu Hurairah, raised to the Prophet: 'When one of you feels something in his belly, then he doubts whether something came out of it or not, then he should not leave the mosque until he hears a sound or perceives an odor.' Commentary: The author is presenting the third principle: certainty will not be removed on the basis of doubt. Its source and origin is a noble hadith found in the Sahihayn. The words of the hadith that the author quoted from Muslim are not found in Muslim; rather the words of Muslim are: from Abdullah ibn Zayd who said: A man complained to the Prophet about something he imagined he felt during prayer. He said: 'He should not leave until he hears a sound or finds an odor.' Question: Doubt cannot coexist with certainty, so what is the meaning of it being removed when it does not exist? Answer: It can be given in this way: the doubt that arises cannot remove the original certainty. Certainty is called the heart's satisfaction with the reality of something; it is said 'the water settled in the basin' when the water stabilizes in the basin. In lexicology, doubt is absolute hesitation. In Usul terminology, the equivalence of both sides of a thing and remaining between two things such that the heart does not incline to either is called doubt. If the heart inclines to one side without discarding the other, it is called zann. And if it discards the other side, it is called ghalib al-zann (preponderant opinion).
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage introduces the third major qa'idah: certainty is not removed by doubt. The author provides the hadith evidence from Sahih Muslim (though noting a discrepancy in wording: the author's version is not exactly that of Muslim, but the meaning is similar). The hadith illustrates that a person who has certainty about being in a state of purity (wudu) does not lose that certainty due to mere doubt about passing wind. He must have sensory evidence (sound or smell) for his doubt to be valid. The author explains that the maxim means that a pre-existing certainty overcomes any later doubt. He then defines key terms: yaqeen is heart's satisfaction about a thing; shakk is equal hesitation between two possibilities; zann is inclination to one side without ruling out the other; ghalib al-zann is preponderant opinion where the other possibility is excluded. These definitions are critical for applying the maxim in fiqh rulings, especially in matters of purity, prayer, and contracts. The author also addresses a potential philosophical objection: if doubt cannot exist with certainty, how can it 'remove' certainty? The answer is that doubt cannot remove an established certainty; rather, the doubt is merely ineffective.

Key Terms
اليقين (al-yaqeen): Heart's satisfaction with the reality of a thing, like water settling in a basin
الشك (al-shakk): Equal hesitation between two possibilities, with the heart not inclining to either
الظن (al-zann): Inclination of the heart to one side without discarding the other
غالب الظن (ghalib al-zann): Preponderant opinion where the heart discards the other possibility

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the third qa'idah, if someone is certain they performed wudu but later doubts whether it broke, what is the ruling?
A) They must repeat wudu.
B) They remain in a state of wudu.
C) They should assume they need wudu to be safe.
D) They must ask someone else.

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'Certainty is not removed by doubt' ensures stability in rulings; only sensory evidence can overturn certainty.

Definitions of Certainty, Belief, Presumption, Doubt, and Delusion
Defining key epistemological terms for the maxim 'Certainty is not removed by doubt'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define yaqin, i'tiqad, zann, shakk, and wahm, and distinguish each based on the strength of evidence and inclination.

Core Idea
Certainty (yaqin) is firm with decisive evidence; doubt (shakk) is equal inclination — the original ruling remains.

In Hanafi legal theory, the strength of one's conviction about a matter directly affects the legal ruling. This passage introduces the five key terms that describe levels of certainty and doubt. Understanding these distinctions is essential for applying the great maxim 'Certainty is not removed by doubt' correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five terms
Locate the five Arabic terms: yaqin, i'tiqad, zann, shakk, wahm. Note their definitions as given in the text.
2
Classify by strength
Order the terms from strongest conviction to weakest: yaqin (certainty) > i'tiqad (belief without decisive evidence) > zann (predominant presumption) > shakk (equal doubt) > wahm (weak delusion).
The text defines shakk as equal inclination; some jurists distinguish equal from predominant, but here shakk covers both.
3
Understand the basis
For each term, identify whether it is based on decisive evidence (dalil qat'i) or not. Yaqin requires it; i'tiqad does not; zann has stronger inclination; shakk is equal; wahm is weaker.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
یقین کے درجہ میں ہے اور اگر دونوں جانبوں میں سے کوئی بھی جانب راجح نہ ہو تو اس کو وہم کہتے ہیں۔ فقہاء کرام کے نزدیک شک تمام ابواب میں لغت کی طرح ہے مساوی اور راج میں کوئی فرق نہیں جیسا کہ نووی کا خیال ہے لیکن یہ احداث کے بارے میں کہا ہے اور دوسرے بہت سے مقامات میں مساوی اور رائج کے پائین فقہاء کرام نے بھی فرق کیا ہے بعض متاخرین کے نزدیک کچھ تبدیلی کے ساتھ دلیل قطعی کی تائید سے قلب کی پختگی کو یقین کہتے ہیں اور دلیل قطعی کی تائید کے بغیر جزم قلب کو اعتقاد کہتے ہیں جیسے عامی کا اعتقاد ۔ اور دو چیزوں میں سے ایک کی تجویز جو دوسرے سے قوی ہوظن کہتے ہیں اور دو چیزوں میں سے ایک کی تجویر جو دوسرے سے کمزور ہو اس کو دو ہم اور دوامروں میں تجویر کہ ایک کو دوسرے پر کوئی فضیلت نہ ہو شک کہتے ہیں۔ پهر شک تین طرح کا ہے: (۱) اصل حرام پر شک طاری ہوا (۲) اصل مباح پر شک طاری ہوا (۳) وہ شک جس کی بنیاد ہی کا پتہ نہ ہو اول کی مثال وہ ند بوحہ بکری جو ایسی جگہ ملے جہاں مسلم اور غیر مسلم کی ملی جلی آبادی ہو وہ حلال نہیں جب تک کہ تحقیق نہ ہو جائے کہ وہ کسی مسلمان کا ذبیحہ ہے اس لئے کہ اس کی اصل حرام ہے جس کی وجہ سے شرعی حلت میں شک ہو گیا اور اگر وہاں غالب اکثریت مسلمانوں کی ہے تو اس ذبیحہ کا کھانا جائز ہے غالب پر عمل کرتے ہوئے جو پاکیزگی کا فائدہ دیتا ہے۔ (۲) دوسرے کی مثال کہ پانی میں تغیر پیدا ہو گیا اور احتمال ہے کہ پانی میں تغیر نجاست کی وجہ سے ہوا یا زیادہ ٹھہرنے کی وجہ سے تو اس سے اصل طہارت پر عمل کرتے ہوئے پاکی حاصل کرنا جائز ہے۔ (۳) تیسرے کی مثال جس کا اکثر مال حرام ہے اس سے معاملہ کرنا کہ جو مال اس سے لیا ہے وہ حلال ہے یا حرام لیکن اس سے معاملہ کرناحرام نہیں ہو سکتا ہے کہ اس نے مال حلال دیا ہو اور حرام نہ دیا ہولیکن حرام میں مبتلا ہونے کے خوف سے اس سے معاملہ کرنا مکروہ ہو گا فتح القدیر میں اسی طرح ہے۔
It is in the level of certainty, and if neither of the two sides is predominant, then it is called delusion. According to the jurists, doubt in all chapters is like linguistic meaning; there is no difference between equal and predominant, as al-Nawawi holds, but he said this regarding hadath. In many other places, the jurists have made a distinction between equal and predominant. According to some later scholars, with some modification, the firmness of the heart supported by decisive evidence is called certainty, and the firmness of the heart without the support of decisive evidence is called belief, like the belief of a common person. The inclination towards one of two things that is stronger than the other is called presumption, and the inclination towards one of two things that is weaker than the other is called delusion, and the inclination between two matters where neither has preference over the other is called doubt. Then doubt is of three types: (1) doubt that overcomes an originally prohibited matter, (2) doubt that overcomes an originally permissible matter, (3) doubt whose basis is not known. For the first, an example is a lost goat found in a place where Muslims and non-Muslims are mixed; it is not permissible until it is investigated that it was slaughtered by a Muslim, because its origin is prohibited, which causes doubt in its legal permissibility. If the majority there are Muslims, then eating it is permissible, acting on the predominant which gives the benefit of purity. For the second, an example is water that has changed, and it is possible that the change was due to impurity or due to standing for a long time; in that case, acting on original purity, it is permissible to use it for purification. For the third, an example is dealing with someone whose wealth is mostly unlawful; the property taken from him may be lawful or unlawful, but dealing with him is not unlawful because he may have given lawful property and not unlawful, but due to fear of falling into the unlawful, dealing with him is disliked. Similarly in Fath al-Qadir.
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage defines key epistemological terms in Hanafi usul and then applies the maxim 'Certainty is not removed by doubt' to three distinct types of doubt. The definitions clarify that certainty (yaqin) is a firm heart supported by definitive evidence, while belief (i'tiqad) lacks such support. Presumption (zann) is stronger inclination, delusion (wahm) is weaker, and doubt (shakk) is equal inclination. The author then classifies doubt into three categories: (1) doubt in an originally prohibited matter, (2) doubt in an originally permissible matter, and (3) doubt with no known original ruling. The examples illustrate that the original ruling prevails unless the doubt is strong enough to overturn it. For instance, a lost goat from a mixed population is presumed forbidden until verification, whereas water that changed but may be due to standing remains pure. The third example shows that dealing with a person whose majority wealth is unlawful is not itself unlawful but disliked.

Key Terms
یقین (yaqin): Firmness of heart supported by definitive evidence (dalil qat'i)
شک (shakk): Equal inclination between two matters with no preference
ظن (zann): Stronger inclination towards one of two possibilities
وہم (wahm): Weaker inclination towards one possibility
اعتقاد (i'tiqad): Firm heart belief without definitive evidence, like a commoner's belief

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which term describes firm conviction without decisive evidence?
A) Yaqin
B) I'tiqad
C) Zann
D) Shakk

Key Takeaway
The five terms — yaqin, i'tiqad, zann, shakk, wahm — form a hierarchy of conviction essential for applying the maxim that certainty is not removed by doubt.

The Three Types of Doubt and the Rule of Original State
Applying the great maxim to three doubt scenarios.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'al-yaqin la yazulu bi al-shakk' to each of three doubt types: doubt in an originally prohibited matter, doubt in an originally permissible matter, and doubt with no known original basis.

Core Idea
When doubt arises, the original ruling (prohibition or permission) prevails until overwhelming evidence overcomes it.

The maxim 'Certainty is not removed by doubt' is one of the five major legal maxims in Hanafi fiqh. This passage illustrates its application to three distinct scenarios: a lost animal, altered water, and dealing with a person of primarily unlawful wealth. Understanding these examples trains the mufti to identify the original state and determine when doubt is ineffective.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three doubt types
From the passage, list the three categories: (1) doubt over an originally prohibited matter, (2) doubt over an originally permissible matter, (3) doubt with no known original ruling.
2
Determine the original state (asl)
For each example, state what the original ruling is before doubt. For the lost goat: prohibition (haram) as origin. For water: purity (taharah) as origin. For dealing with a person: permissibility (ibahah) as origin.
The original state is always presumed certain.
3
Apply the maxim
Since yaqin (original ruling) is certain, it is not removed by shakk (doubt). Thus the original ruling remains unless the doubt is resolved by preponderance (ghalabah).
In the goat example, if Muslims are majority, their predominant probability (zann ghalib) overcomes the original prohibition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
یقین کے درجہ میں ہے اور اگر دونوں جانبوں میں سے کوئی بھی جانب راجح نہ ہو تو اس کو وہم کہتے ہیں۔ فقہاء کرام کے نزدیک شک تمام ابواب میں لغت کی طرح ہے مساوی اور راج میں کوئی فرق نہیں جیسا کہ نووی کا خیال ہے لیکن یہ احداث کے بارے میں کہا ہے اور دوسرے بہت سے مقامات میں مساوی اور رائج کے پائین فقہاء کرام نے بھی فرق کیا ہے بعض متاخرین کے نزدیک کچھ تبدیلی کے ساتھ دلیل قطعی کی تائید سے قلب کی پختگی کو یقین کہتے ہیں اور دلیل قطعی کی تائید کے بغیر جزم قلب کو اعتقاد کہتے ہیں جیسے عامی کا اعتقاد ۔ اور دو چیزوں میں سے ایک کی تجویز جو دوسرے سے قوی ہوظن کہتے ہیں اور دو چیزوں میں سے ایک کی تجویر جو دوسرے سے کمزور ہو اس کو دو ہم اور دوامروں میں تجویر کہ ایک کو دوسرے پر کوئی فضیلت نہ ہو شک کہتے ہیں۔ پهر شک تین طرح کا ہے: (۱) اصل حرام پر شک طاری ہوا (۲) اصل مباح پر شک طاری ہوا (۳) وہ شک جس کی بنیاد ہی کا پتہ نہ ہو اول کی مثال وہ ند بوحہ بکری جو ایسی جگہ ملے جہاں مسلم اور غیر مسلم کی ملی جلی آبادی ہو وہ حلال نہیں جب تک کہ تحقیق نہ ہو جائے کہ وہ کسی مسلمان کا ذبیحہ ہے اس لئے کہ اس کی اصل حرام ہے جس کی وجہ سے شرعی حلت میں شک ہو گیا اور اگر وہاں غالب اکثریت مسلمانوں کی ہے تو اس ذبیحہ کا کھانا جائز ہے غالب پر عمل کرتے ہوئے جو پاکیزگی کا فائدہ دیتا ہے۔ (۲) دوسرے کی مثال کہ پانی میں تغیر پیدا ہو گیا اور احتمال ہے کہ پانی میں تغیر نجاست کی وجہ سے ہوا یا زیادہ ٹھہرنے کی وجہ سے تو اس سے اصل طہارت پر عمل کرتے ہوئے پاکی حاصل کرنا جائز ہے۔ (۳) تیسرے کی مثال جس کا اکثر مال حرام ہے اس سے معاملہ کرنا کہ جو مال اس سے لیا ہے وہ حلال ہے یا حرام لیکن اس سے معاملہ کرناحرام نہیں ہو سکتا ہے کہ اس نے مال حلال دیا ہو اور حرام نہ دیا ہولیکن حرام میں مبتلا ہونے کے خوف سے اس سے معاملہ کرنا مکروہ ہو گا فتح القدیر میں اسی طرح ہے۔
It is in the level of certainty, and if neither of the two sides is predominant, then it is called delusion. According to the jurists, doubt in all chapters is like linguistic meaning; there is no difference between equal and predominant, as al-Nawawi holds, but he said this regarding hadath. In many other places, the jurists have made a distinction between equal and predominant. According to some later scholars, with some modification, the firmness of the heart supported by decisive evidence is called certainty, and the firmness of the heart without the support of decisive evidence is called belief, like the belief of a common person. The inclination towards one of two things that is stronger than the other is called presumption, and the inclination towards one of two things that is weaker than the other is called delusion, and the inclination between two matters where neither has preference over the other is called doubt. Then doubt is of three types: (1) doubt that overcomes an originally prohibited matter, (2) doubt that overcomes an originally permissible matter, (3) doubt whose basis is not known. For the first, an example is a lost goat found in a place where Muslims and non-Muslims are mixed; it is not permissible until it is investigated that it was slaughtered by a Muslim, because its origin is prohibited, which causes doubt in its legal permissibility. If the majority there are Muslims, then eating it is permissible, acting on the predominant which gives the benefit of purity. For the second, an example is water that has changed, and it is possible that the change was due to impurity or due to standing for a long time; in that case, acting on original purity, it is permissible to use it for purification. For the third, an example is dealing with someone whose wealth is mostly unlawful; the property taken from him may be lawful or unlawful, but dealing with him is not unlawful because he may have given lawful property and not unlawful, but due to fear of falling into the unlawful, dealing with him is disliked. Similarly in Fath al-Qadir.
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage defines key epistemological terms in Hanafi usul and then applies the maxim 'Certainty is not removed by doubt' to three distinct types of doubt. The definitions clarify that certainty (yaqin) is a firm heart supported by definitive evidence, while belief (i'tiqad) lacks such support. Presumption (zann) is stronger inclination, delusion (wahm) is weaker, and doubt (shakk) is equal inclination. The author then classifies doubt into three categories: (1) doubt in an originally prohibited matter, (2) doubt in an originally permissible matter, and (3) doubt with no known original ruling. The examples illustrate that the original ruling prevails unless the doubt is strong enough to overturn it. For instance, a lost goat from a mixed population is presumed forbidden until verification, whereas water that changed but may be due to standing remains pure. The third example shows that dealing with a person whose majority wealth is unlawful is not itself unlawful but disliked.

Key Terms
یقین (yaqin): Firmness of heart supported by definitive evidence (dalil qat'i)
شک (shakk): Equal inclination between two matters with no preference
ظن (zann): Stronger inclination towards one of two possibilities
وہم (wahm): Weaker inclination towards one possibility
اعتقاد (i'tiqad): Firm heart belief without definitive evidence, like a commoner's belief
غالب (ghalib): Predominant probability; weightier side with strong evidence

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on water that has changed in color but it is unclear whether the change is from impurity or from standing for a long time?
A) It is impure
B) It remains pure
C) It is doubtful so avoid it
D) It requires investigation

Key Takeaway
The correct application of 'Certainty is not removed by doubt' requires first identifying the original state (asl) and then assessing whether the doubt is equal or outweighed by predominant probability.

Defending the Maxim: Certainty and Specification by Solitary Reports
Answering an objection to the qawida using usul reasoning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi interpretation of the maxim 'al-yaqinu la yazulu bi al-shakk' to reconcile it with the specification (takhsis) of the Quran by a solitary report (khabar wahid).

Core Idea
'Certainty' in the maxim means a settled ruling, not absolute evidence; specification is clarification, not removal.

The maxim 'Certainty is not removed by doubt' is a foundational principle in Hanafi legal theory. However, a potential objection arises: how can the Quran, which is definitive (qat'i), be specified by a solitary report (khabar wahid), which is speculative (zanni)? Does this not violate the maxim? This lesson explores the Hanafi response, which clarifies the scope of 'certainty' and the nature of specification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Objection
State the apparent conflict between the maxim and the practice of takhsis by khabar wahid. The objection claims that a speculative report is removing a certain ruling.
The objector assumes 'certainty' means absolute evidential certainty.
2
Understand Key Terms
Distinguish between 'certainty' in the maxim (a ruling that has been established) and epistemological certainty (the strength of evidence).
Hanafi usul defines yaqin here as a settled ruling, not qat'i evidence.
3
Analyze the Hanafi Response
Recognize that both the Quran and khabar wahid are equally binding in practice (wujub al-'amal). Specification does not remove the general ruling but clarifies its scope. Thus, the maxim is not violated.
The response cites Qawa'id al-Zarkashi and the hashiah of Hamawi.
4
Examine Conditions and Limits
Note that specification by khabar wahid is not absolute; it has conditions and is not universally permissible. The maxim remains intact as a default principle.
See Usul al-Hadith and Usul al-Tafsir for details.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سوال: اليقين لا يزول بالشک ایک اصولی مسئلہ سے ٹوٹ جاتا ہے اور خبر واحد سے قرآن کا شیخ ہے خبر احد فنی ہے اور قرآن قطعی سے لیکن یہاں پر قطعی اور یقینی ملنی چیز سے ٹوٹ اور زائل ہو رہی ہے۔ جواب یہ ہے کہ یقین سے قطعیت مراد نہیں بلکہ پہلے ثابت شدہ حکم اپنے ہی جیسے حکم سے مرتفع ہو جاتا ہے اور نص قرآن اور خبر واحد وجوب عمل کے سلسلہ میں دونوں برابر ہیں اور احکام میں اس قدر فرق کافی ہے قواعد زرکشی میں اسی طرح ہے ظاہر خبر واحد سے مطلقاً قرآن شیخ جائز نہیں جس کی تفصیل اصول حدیث اور اصول تفسیر میں دیکھی جاسکتی ہے یہ پوری تفصیل علامہ حموی نے الاشباہ کے حاشیہ میں نقل کی ہے طلبہ کی آسانی کے لئے اس کا ترجمہ نقل کر دیا گیا ہے۔
Question: The principle 'Certainty is not removed by doubt' is broken by an usuli issue: the specification of the Quran by a solitary report. Khabar wahid is speculative, while the Quran is definitive. Here, the definitive and certain thing is being broken and removed. Answer: By certainty, decisiveness is not meant; rather, a previously established ruling can only be removed by a ruling of its own type. The text of the Quran and a solitary report are equal in terms of mandatory action, and this difference in rulings is sufficient. Similarly in Qawa'id al-Zarkashi. It is not permissible to specify the Quran by a solitary report absolutely; the details can be seen in Usul al-Hadith and Usul al-Tafsir. This entire detail was transmitted by Allamah Hamawi in his hashiah on al-Ashbah, and for the convenience of students, its translation is given here.
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage addresses a potential objection to the maxim 'Certainty is not removed by doubt': how can it be that a solitary report (khabar wahid) specifies a definitive text of the Quran? The objector argues that this seems to violate the maxim because a speculative report is removing a certain ruling. The Hanafi response clarifies that 'certainty' in the maxim does not mean absolute epistemological certainty but refers to a previously established ruling that can only be removed by another ruling of equal or stronger authority. In terms of obligatory action (wujub al-'amal), both the Quran and khabar wahid are binding, so specification is not a removal but a limitation of scope. The answer notes that this is consistent with the position in Qawa'id al-Zarkashi and that absolute specification of Quran by khabar wahid is not permissible without conditions. The discussion is a classic example of how the Hanafi school harmonizes usul principles with specific legal evidences.

Key Terms
یقین (yaqin): In this context, the term refers to a previously established ruling, not absolute epistemological certainty.
خبر واحد (khabar wahid): A solitary report that is speculative (zanni) in transmission but still binding in practice.
تخصیص (takhsis): Specification of a general text by a specific evidence, not removal of the general ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How does the Hanafi response reconcile the maxim 'Certainty is not removed by doubt' with the practice of specifying the Quran by a solitary report?
A) By denying that solitary reports are valid for specification.
B) By stating that certainty in the maxim refers to a previously established ruling, not epistemological certainty, and that both sources are equally binding for action.
C) By arguing that the Quran is no longer definitive after specification.
D) By claiming that the maxim only applies to matters of worship, not to texts.

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'Certainty is not removed by doubt' does not prohibit specification by solitary reports because 'certainty' means a settled ruling, not absolute evidence, and specification is clarification, not removal.
