Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the Hanafi legal maxim 'al-asl al-adam' (the default is non-existence) and its qualification. The author first presents several fiqh examples where the default of non-existence determines whose statement is accepted in disputes: in usurpation, option of condition, option of sight, and change of merchandise. Then he introduces an important refinement: the maxim is not absolute. It applies only to accidental attributes (sifaat 'aaridah) – those that can be acquired or lost – but not to original attributes (sifaat asliyyah) that are inherent in the creation of a thing, where the default is existence. He then provides multiple applications of this distinction: in buying a slave with a condition of being a baker or writer (accidental, so buyer's claim of non-existence is accepted), buying a slave-girl with condition of virginity (original, so seller's claim of existence is accepted), and cases of conditional emancipation based on these attributes. The passage ends by introducing a new qawida: 'al-asl idafat al-hadith ila aqrab awqatihi' (the default is to attribute an event to its nearest time), giving the example of najasah found on a garment after prayer. This section is incomplete in this window and continues elsewhere.

Key Terms
الأصل (al-asl): The default legal presumption or starting point in absence of evidence.
الصفات العارضة (al-sifaat al-'aaridah): Accidental attributes that are not inherent in a thing's creation, such as skills or temporary conditions.
الصفات الأصلية (al-sifaat al-asliyyah): Original attributes that are part of the creation of a thing, such as virginity, limbs, or innate qualities.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A master says 'Every baker slave of mine is free.' A slave claims he is a baker; the master denies. Who is believed?
A) The slave, because he claims freedom.
B) The master, because being a baker is an accidental attribute and default is non-existence.
C) The master, because the master is the owner.
D) The slave, because the default is existence for all attributes.

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'al-asl al-adam' is not absolute; it applies only to accidental attributes, while original attributes default to existence.

Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims
intermediate
6 min
Rule of Attributing Events to the Nearest Time (iḍāfat al-ḥādiṯ ilā aqrab awqātihī)
Explains the rule, its applications, and an exception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim iḍāfat al-ḥādiṯ ilā aqrab awqātihī to furuat and identify when taḥkīm al-ḥāl overrides it.

Core Idea
When an event's timing is disputed, attribute it to the nearest time — unless the current state proves otherwise.

This lesson unpacks a powerful Hanafi maxim that resolves disputes over timing of events: iḍāfat al-ḥādiṯ ilā aqrab awqātihī. Through concrete furuat in sales, divorce, and inheritance, you will see how the maxim operates and when it is set aside by the principle of taḥkīm al-ḥāl, which looks at the present state to infer the past. Understanding this tool is essential for any mufti tasked with issuing fatwas on disputed temporal claims.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its underlying support
Begin by articulating the maxim 'Attributing the event to the nearest time' and connect it to the supporting qāʿidah al-aṣl baqāʾ mā kān ʿalā mā kān (the default is continuity). This provides the epistemological foundation.
The maxim draws on istiṣḥāb al-ḥāl — the presumption that a state continues until proven otherwise.
2
Apply the maxim to the slave eye case
Consider the case where a slave's eye is poked and the timing is disputed between buyer and poking party. Attribute the injury to the nearest time, which is the buyer's ownership (the present state). The buyer's statement is accepted, and he takes compensation.
3
Apply the maxim to the divorce case
A wife claims divorce during terminal illness (fārr); heirs claim healthy divorce. The nearest time to the death is the illness, so the wife's statement is accepted and she inherits.
This illustrates the maxim's role in protecting the wife from fraudulent divorces.
4
Identify the exception: the dhimmi case
When a dhimmi dies and his widow claims she remained non-Muslim at death, but the heirs claim she converted earlier, the maxim would accept her statement (nearest time non-Muslim). However, the majority Hanafi position overrides this via taḥkīm al-ḥāl: since she is Muslim now, the cause of deprivation (difference of religion) is present, so it is deemed to have existed earlier. The heirs' statement is accepted.
Imam Zufar held to the general rule, but the majority departed due to istiḥsān based on the current state.
5
Derive the ruling for the heir's acknowledgment case
An heir acknowledges a debt and dies. The recipient claims healthy acknowledgment (full effect); heirs claim sick acknowledgment (limited to one-third). The nearest time to death is illness, so the heirs' statement is accepted. The recipient must provide evidence or may demand an oath.
This shows the maxim applied to iqrār, balancing protection of heirs' rights.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمِنْهَا لَوْ كَانَ فِي يَدِ رَجُلٍ عَبْدٌ فَقَالَ رَجُلٌ فَقَاتُ عَيْنَهُ وَهُوْ فِيْ مِلْكِ الْبَائِعِ وَ قَالَ الْمُشْتَرِى فَقَاتَهُ وَ هُوَ فِي مِلْكِى فَالْقَوْلُ لِلْمُشْتَرِى فَيَأْخُذُ إِرْشَهُ وَمِنْهَا ادَّعَتْ أَنَّ زَوْجَهَا أبَانَهَا فِي الْمَرْضِ وَصَارَ فَارًا فَتَرِتُ وَ قَالَتِ الْوَرْثَةُ أَبَانَهَا فِي صِحَتِهِ فَلَا تَرِتُ كَانَ الْقُوْلُ قَوْلَهَا فَتَرِثُ وَخَرْجَ عَنْ هَذَا الْأَصْلِ مَسَألَةُ الْكَيْرِ مِنْ مَسَائِلَ شَتَّى مِنَ الْقَضَاءِ. وَإِنَّ مَاتَ ذِمِّيٌّ فَقَالَتِ زَوْجَتُهُ أَسْلَمْتُ بَعْدَ مَوْتِهِ وَ قَالَتِ الْوَرَثَةُ أَسْلَمْتُ قَبْلَ مَوْتِهِ فَالْقَوْلُ لَهُمْ مَعْ أَنَّ الْأَصْلَ الْمَذْكُورَ يَقْتَضِى أَنْ يَكُوْنَ الْقَوْلُ قَوْلَهَا وَبِهِ قَالَ زُفَةٌ وَ إِنَّمَا خَرَجُوْا عَنْ هَذِهِ الْقَاعِدَةِ فِيْهَا لِأَجْلِ تَحْكِيمِ الْحَالِ وَهُوَ أَنَّ سَبَبَ الْحِرْمَانِ ثَابِتٌ فِي الْحَالِ فَيَثْبُتُ فِيْمَا مَضى. وَمِمَّا فَرَّعَتُهُ عَلَى الْأَصْلِ مَا فِي الْيَتِيْمَةِ وَغَيْرِهَا وَلَوْ أَقَرَّ الوَارِتُ ثُمَّ مَاتَ فَقَالَ الْمُقِرُّلَهُ أَقَرَّ فِي الصَّحَّةِ وَقَالَتِ الْوَرَثَةُ فِي مَرْضِهِ فَالْقَوْلُ قَوْلُ الْوَرَثَةِ وَالْبَيِّنَةُ بَيْنَةُ الْمَقِرلَهُ وَإِنْ لَمْ يُقِمْ بَيْنَتَهُ وَأَرَادَ إِسْتِحْلَافَهُمْ فَلَهُ ذَلِكَ إِنْتَهَى. وَمِمَّا فَرَّعْتَهُ عَلَى هَذَا الْأَصْلِ قَوْلُهُمْ لَوْمَاتَ مُسْلِمٌ وَتَحْتَهُ نَصْرَانِيَّةٌ فَجَاءَتْ مُسْلِمَةٌ بَعْدَ مَوْتِهِ وَقَالَتْ أَسْلَمْتُ قَبْلَ مَوْتِهِ وَقَالَتِ الْوَرَثَةُ أَسْلَمْتُ بَعْدَ مَوْتِهِ فَالْقَوْلُ لَهُمْ كَمَا ذَكَرَهُ الزَّيْلَعِيُّ فِي مَسَائِلَ شَى.
And among them: if in the hand of a man there is a slave, and a man says, 'I poked out his eye while he was in the ownership of the seller,' and the buyer says, 'You poked his eye while he was in my ownership,' then the statement is the buyer's, so he takes the compensation (arsh). And among them: a woman claimed that her husband divorced her during illness and became a fugitive (to deprive her of inheritance), so she inherits; and the heirs said, 'He divorced her while healthy, so she does not inherit.' The statement is hers, so she inherits. And excluded from this principle is the issue of the treasure from the various judicial issues. And if a dhimmi dies, and his wife says, 'I embraced Islam after his death,' and the heirs say, 'You embraced Islam before his death,' then the statement is theirs, even though the aforementioned principle would require her statement to be accepted. And it is the view of Zufar. They only departed from this rule in this case due to considering the current state (tahkim al-hal), which is that the cause of deprivation is established in the present, so it is established with respect to the past. And among what I have derived from this principle is that which is in al-Yatimah and others: if the heir acknowledges and then dies, and the one acknowledged for says, 'He acknowledged while healthy,' and the heirs say, 'While ill,' then the statement is the heirs', and the evidence is that of the one acknowledged for; and if he does not provide his evidence and wants to have them take an oath, he may do so. End. And among what is derived from this principle is their statement: if a Muslim dies with a Christian woman under his marriage, and she comes as a Muslim after his death and says, 'I embraced Islam before his death,' and the heirs say, 'You embraced Islam after his death,' then the statement is theirs, as al-Zayla'i mentioned in various issues.
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of the legal maxim 'attributing an event to its nearest time' (iḍāfat al-ḥādiṯ ilā aqrab awqātihī). The author introduces it by presenting three applications and one exception, then additional derivations. 

The principle: When a disputed event could have occurred at different times, the law attributes it to the time closest to the present, because the default is continuity (istiṣḥāb). 

First application: A slave is in someone's possession. One person claims he poked the slave's eye while it was still owned by the seller; the buyer claims the poking happened under his ownership. The dispute is about timing. Applying the rule, the nearest time is the buyer's ownership (since the slave is currently in his possession), so the buyer's statement is believed, and he takes the compensation. 

Second application: A wife claims her husband divorced her during his terminal illness, making him a 'fārr' (one who flees from inheritance), so she inherits. The heirs claim the divorce happened while healthy, so she does not inherit. The nearest time to the present is the husband's death, and the wife's claim that it was during illness is closer to that time. Thus, her statement is accepted, and she inherits. 

Exception: The 'issue of the treasure' (mas'alat al-kanz) from various judicial problems. This is a case where a dhimmi dies and his widow claims she was still non-Muslim at his death (so she inherits as a dhimmi), but the heirs claim she had already converted to Islam before his death, which would bar inheritance due to difference of religion. According to the principle, her statement should be believed because it attributes the event (her non-Muslim status) to the nearest time (his death). Imam Zufar held this view. However, the majority of Hanafis departed from the rule here due to 'taḥkīm al-ḥāl' (consideration of the current state): since the cause of deprivation (difference of religion) is currently established (she is Muslim now), it is presumed to have existed in the past as well, unless she provides evidence otherwise. This overrides the general rule. 

Additional derivations from the principle: 
- An heir makes an acknowledgment (iqrār) then dies. The recipient says the acknowledgment was made while the heir was healthy; the heirs say it was made during illness (which limits the validity to one-third of the estate). The nearest time to the death is illness, so the heirs' statement is accepted, and the burden of proof is on the recipient. If the recipient has no evidence, he can demand an oath from the heirs. 
- A Muslim dies leaving a Christian wife. She later becomes Muslim and claims she converted before his death (so she inherits as Muslim). The heirs claim she converted after death (so no inheritance). The nearest time to his death is the death itself; but since she is currently Muslim, the cause of deprivation (difference of religion) is currently absent. However, the Hanafi position is that since at the time of death she was presumably non-Muslim (given the marriage contract), the default is that she remained so, so the heirs' statement is accepted. This aligns with al-Zayla'i's report. 

Throughout, the author demonstrates how the principle operates and when it is overridden by other considerations.

Key Terms
إضافة الحادث إلى أقرب أوقاته (iḍāfat al-ḥādiṯ ilā aqrab awqātihī): When a disputed event's timing is uncertain, regard it as occurring at the time closest to the present or the time relevant to the dispute.
تحكيم الحال (taḥkīm al-ḥāl): Preferring the current state (ḥāl) as evidence to determine a past event, especially when the cause of a legal effect is presently observable.
فارّ (fārr): A husband who divorces his wife during terminal illness to prevent her from inheriting; he is considered fraudulent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the dhimmi case (exception), why do the majority of Hanafis reject the rule of attributing to nearest time?
A) Because the rule only applies to acts of worship.
B) Because the current state (wife is Muslim now) indicates the cause of deprivation existed earlier.
C) Because the wife is a liar.
D) Because Imam Zufar's opinion is stronger.

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'iḍāfat al-ḥādiṯ ilā aqrab awqātihī' resolves timing disputes by default, but must yield to taḥkīm al-ḥāl when the present state reveals the cause of a legal change.

Applying the Maxim of Attributing Events to the Nearest Time
Applying the maxim of attributing events to nearest time

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim 'Idafat al-hadith ila aqrab awqatih' to resolve timing disputes in inheritance cases involving conversion.

Core Idea
When timing of an event is disputed, the closest plausible time is presumed unless proven otherwise.

This lesson examines a classic Hanafi ruling that applies the maxim 'Idafat al-hadith ila aqrab awqatih' (attributing an event to its nearest time). The scenario involves a Muslim man who dies leaving a Christian wife. After his death, she converts to Islam, but a dispute arises over the exact timing of her conversion—before or after his death—which affects inheritance rights. The Hanafi rule gives preference to the heirs' claim that she converted after death, based on this powerful qawida.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed event and its possible timings
The event is the wife's conversion to Islam. The two possible timings are: (a) before the husband's death, (b) after the husband's death. This timing directly affects her inheritance (she inherits if Muslim at his death) and whether she must observe iddah as a Muslim widow.
In Hanafi law, a non-Muslim wife does not inherit from a Muslim husband; conversion before death would change that.
2
State the governing maxim
The maxim 'إضافة الحادث إلى أقرب أوقاته' instructs that when the precise moment of an event is uncertain, we attribute it to the nearest possible time (the most recent moment) from the perspective of the present. This is a foundational qawida in Hanafi usul al-fiqh.
This maxim is often invoked alongside the principle that the original state (non-Muslim) continues until proven otherwise.
3
Apply the maxim to the scenario
The conversion is the new event (hadith). At the time of dispute, the nearest possible time for this conversion is after the husband's death (the most recent point). The wife's claim of an earlier conversion is a claim of a more distant time. Unless she brings clear evidence, the maxim dictates that we adopt the nearest time—i.e., after death.
The heirs benefit from this maxim because they assert the later (nearest) time.
4
Identify the underlying presumption
The maxim implements a broader rule: the original state (asl) is presumed to continue until a change is proven. Here, the wife's original state is non-Muslim; her conversion is a change. The change is presumed to have occurred at the latest plausible moment, not earlier, absent proof.
This parallels the principle 'al-asl baqa' ma kana 'ala ma kana' (the original state remains).
5
Derive the ruling
Since the conversion is attributed to the nearest time (after death), the heirs' statement that she converted after death is authoritative. Therefore, she is treated as having died a non-Muslim widow, and the heirs' inheritance shares are calculated accordingly. Her claim of earlier conversion is not accepted without evidence.
This ruling is from the Hanafi school and is the mufta bihi (operative) position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و مما فرعته على هذا الأصل قولهم لومات مسلم وتحته نصرانية: کسی مسلم کے نکاح میں نصرانی عورت تھی اس کے انتقال کے بعد وہ اسلام لے آئی اس کے بعد وہ نصران یہ دعوی کرتی ہے کہ میں اس کی موت کے پہلے ہی اسلام لے آئی تھی جبکہ ورثاء کہتے ہیں کہ تو اس کی موت کے بعد اسلام لائی ہے تو اس صورت میں ورثاء کا قول معتبر ہوگا کیونکہ ان کا قول اقرب اوقات کی طرف منسوب ہے۔
And from what I derived upon this principle is their statement: If a Muslim dies and under him is a Christian woman, after his death she accepts Islam, then she claims that she had accepted Islam before his death, while the heirs say that she accepted Islam after his death. In this case, the heirs' statement is authoritative because their statement is attributed to the nearest time.
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage demonstrates an application of the maxim 'إضافة الحادث إلى أقرب أوقاته' (attributing a new event to its nearest time). The case: a Muslim man dies leaving a Christian wife. After his death, she converts to Islam, but then disputes arise about when she converted—before or after his death. The heirs claim she converted after death, which would affect inheritance. According to the Hanafi school, the heirs' word is taken because the conversion is a recent event, and it is attributed to the nearest possible time, which is after death, unless there is evidence to the contrary. This upholds the principle that the original state (non-Muslim) is presumed to continue until proven otherwise.

Key Terms
إضافة الحادث إلى أقرب أوقاته (Idāfat al-ḥādith ilā aqrab awqātih): Attributing a new event (ḥādith) to its nearest possible time for legal purposes.
ورثة (wurathā'): Heirs (plural); in this context, the relatives who inherit from the deceased.
نصرانية (naṣrāniyya): A Christian woman; a woman from the People of the Book (Ahl al-Kitab).

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Because the heirs are the legal representatives of the deceased

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi maxim 'Idafat al-hadith ila aqrab awqatih' provides a default rule in timing disputes: without evidence, the event is considered to have occurred at the nearest moment, preserving the original state.

Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims

The Maxim of Attributing Events to the Nearest Time and Its Applications
Explaining and applying the maxim with multiple furuat and disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'إضافة الحادث إلى أقرب أوقاته' to determine liability in disputed events, and identify when it is overridden by the principle of innocence.

Core Idea
An event is attributed to its nearest time, but the principle of innocence overrides when liability is denied.

The Hanafi legal tradition is rich with maxims that guide jurists in resolving disputes over facts. One such maxim is 'Attributing an event to its nearest time,' which helps determine which party's claim is stronger when the timing of an action is contested. However, this maxim is not absolute; it must be balanced against the stronger presumption of innocence. In this lesson, we will explore the maxim's application through several cases and understand its limitation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the maxim and its scope
The maxim attributes an event to the nearest possible time when the timing is disputed. It applies in cases of guarantee, claims, and confessions.
2
Examine the first application: master and freed slave
Analyze the case from al-Nihayah: master cuts slave's hand, disputes before/after emancipation. See how Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf differ from Imam Muhammad.
The nearest time (freedom) supports the slave's claim.
3
Analyse the exceptions: when innocence prevails
Review cases from al-Majma' where the nearest time would impose liability but the principle of innocence (براءة الذمة) overrides, because the defendant is a denier of liability.
The maxim of innocence is stronger.
4
Evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute and the mufta bihi
Identify the relied-upon position for fatwa: in the master-slave case, the view of the two shaykhs (slave's word accepted) is mufta bihi; in the harbi cases, no liability per al-Majma'.
The mufti must carefully assess who is the claimant and denier.

ضامن ہوگا۔ او بقطع يد معتقه: آقا نے دعویٰ کیا کہ میں نے زید کا ہاتھ اس وقت کاٹا تھا جب وہ میرا غلام تھا بعد میں میں نے آزاد کر دیا زید کہتا ہے کہ تو نے میرا ہا تھ آزاد کرنے کے بعد کا نا تھا لہذا تجھ پر ضمان واجب ہے تو اگر چہ بعد الحق
It will be. The principle is innocence of liability and the principle is the word of the trustee. Similarly in the issue of produce: the master said to the slave that the income was from the slave, the income has been lost, so the master's word is accepted although it is attributed to the most distant time because the master is denier of guarantee, and this case is excluded from the rule. If the income was in the master's possession, the master would not be believed. And from what agrees with the principle is that in al-Nihayah: if he freed a female slave then said to her, 'I cut your hand while you were my slave,' and she said, 'You cut it while I was free,' then her word is accepted. And similarly in everything he took from her according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. He mentioned it before the chapter of testimonies. And these cases require a precise look to differentiate between them. Translation: And in accordance with the principle, the case from al-Nihayah is that if after freeing a female slave, he said to her, 'I cut your hand when you were my slave,' and she said, 'You cut it while I was free,' then her word is accepted. And according to the two shaykhs, the same ruling applies to everything he took from her. The author of al-Nihayah mentioned it shortly before the chapter of testimonies. And these cases require a deep insight to differentiate between them. Explanation: According to the earlier principle of attributing the event to the nearest time, the case from al-Nihayah is also that if the master, after freeing his female slave, said, 'I cut your hand when you were a slave,' meaning the master is claiming no compensation, and the slave says, 'You cut my hand when I was free,' meaning the slave is claiming compensation, then by attributing to the nearest time, the slave's word is accepted and compensation is due from the master because freedom is nearer. Similarly, for every item the master took from the slave, if he says, 'I took that item from you while you were a slave,' and the slave says, 'You took it after freeing,' then according to the two shaykhs, based on attributing the event to the nearest time, the slave's word is accepted and compensation is due from the master. Whereas according to Imam Muhammad, the master's word is accepted and no compensation is due from the master. His statement: 'And these cases require a precise look.' Some said: The author wrote something in the commentary that could answer the difference between the issue of freeing a male slave and the issue of a female slave; the author did not need to respond by referring to al-Nihayah. That is: when the confector attributes his confession to a state that is completely contrary to guarantee, then nothing from what we mentioned in the commentary becomes binding. This was refuted: the state of slavery is not a state in which guarantee is completely negated, because if the slave is authorized, he is liable for value. Similarly, if he destroys a pledged slave, he is also liable, as is known from the texts. Similarly, the state of judgment is not completely contrary to guarantee because if the judge becomes a protector of injustice (habitual), he will be liable. Therefore, the condition of 'completely' is not necessary; rather, attributing the act to a state that is contrary to guarantee is absolutely sufficient. Thus, in al-Bahr al-Raiq, the condition 'completely' that the author set is not mentioned there. It is known that he is not pleased with it, and he said these cases require a precise look. And the authorship of al-Ashbah is later than the supplement of al-Bahr, so in al-Ashbah the condition 'completely' is not present; thus the author's chosen view is this. And in al-Majma' from the chapter of confession: if a harbi who converted to Islam confessed to taking property before Islam, or to destroying wine after it, or a Muslim to taking property of a harbi in dar al-harb, or to cutting the hand of his freed slave before emancipation, and they denied him in attribution, then he gave a fatwa of no guarantee in all cases, end. And the two shaykhs say he guarantees. Translation: And in al-Majma' from the chapter of confession: a harbi who became Muslim confessed to taking property before Islam, or to destroying wine after it, or a Muslim to taking property of a harbi in dar al-harb, or to cutting the hand of his freed slave before emancipation, and they denied him in the attribution, then they gave a fatwa of no guarantee in all cases. And the two shaykhs say he guarantees. Explanation: If a harbi, after embracing Islam, confesses that he took someone's property before Islam, and that person says he took it after Islam, so he must return the property or pay compensation, then the time after Islam is nearer, but here, based on the principle of innocence of liability, the ruling is no guarantee. And according to the two shaykhs, he is liable. Or destroying wine: if a harbi claims after Islam that he destroyed someone's wine after Islam, and people say no, you destroyed the wine before embracing Islam, so compensation is due on you, then in this case the harbi's word is accepted because he is denier of liability, while the two shaykhs hold liability. Or a Muslim taking property of a harbi: a Muslim claimed he took a harbi's property in dar al-harb, but people said he took it in dar al-Islam, then in this case his statement is not accepted and liability will not apply, while the two shaykhs say he is liable. Or cutting the hand of his freed slave: the master claimed he cut Zayd's hand when he was his slave, then he freed him; Zayd says you cut my hand after freeing, so compensation is due on you. Then even though the latest time...
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of the Hanafi legal maxim 'Attributing an event to the nearest time' (إضافة الحادث إلى أقرب أوقاته). The author presents several applications of this maxim from various fiqh works, along with scholarly differences within the Hanafi school. The central principle is that when an event is attributed to a time, the nearest time is taken as the reference. However, this maxim is not absolute; it yields to the stronger principle of 'innocence of liability' (براءة الذمة) when the claimant is denying liability. The passage begins with a ruling from al-Nihayah: if a master cuts his female slave's hand and then frees her, and there is a dispute about whether the cutting occurred before or after emancipation, the slave's word is accepted (according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) because freedom is the nearer state. The same applies to any item the master took. Imam Muhammad disagrees, holding that the master's word prevails. The author then critically examines a comment in a earlier commentary that attempted to distinguish between different cases by requiring the state to be 'completely' contrary to guarantee. He rejects this condition, showing that states like slavery or judgment are not completely contrary to guarantee. He provides examples from al-Majma' where the principle of innocence overrides the nearest-time rule: e.g., a harbi converting to Islam confessing to taking property before Islam—the nearest time would be after Islam, but he is not liable because he denies liability. The two shaykhs (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold him liable in some of these cases. Thus, this passage demonstrates the interplay of maxims and the need for precise reasoning in fiqh.

Key Terms
إضافة الحادث إلى أقرب أوقاته (Idhaafat al-haadith ilaa aqrab awqaatihi): A legal maxim that attributes an event to the nearest possible time, especially in disputes over when an action occurred.
براءة الذمة (Baraa'at al-dhimmah): The legal presumption that a person is free from liability unless proven otherwise.
منكر ضمان (Munkir damaan): A person who denies being liable for compensation or guarantee.
تكذيب في الإسناد (Takdheeb fi al-isnaad): Denial (by others) of the attribution of an event to a particular time.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case of a master cutting his female slave's hand, according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, whose statement is accepted and why?
A) The master's statement, because he is the owner.
B) The slave's statement, because freedom is the nearest time.
C) The master's statement, because he denies liability.
D) The slave's statement, because she is the claimant.

Key Takeaway
The maxim of attributing events to the nearest time is a key tool in Hanafi iftaa, but it must be balanced against the stronger principle of innocence.

Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims
intermediate
10 min
Default Rule for Things: Permissibility, Prohibition, or Suspension?
Qa'ida presenting three views on the default ruling for things before revelation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi views on the default ruling for things before revelation and identify which view is mufta bihi (operative for fatwa).

Core Idea
The default ruling for things before revelation is debated, but later fatwa favours permissibility.

When a new issue arises that is not directly addressed in the Qur'an or Sunnah, a mufti must decide on its ruling. This qa'ida (legal maxim) guides that decision by asking: what is the default legal status of things before any revelation? The Hanafi school presents three distinct views, each with its own reasoning and scholarly support. Understanding this difference is essential for accurate fatwa drafting.

Guided Path
1
Define the scope of the qa'ida
Recognize that this maxim applies to things (actions or objects) before revelation (shar') provides a ruling. It does not apply to matters already ruled by clear texts.
The term 'ashya' includes both acts and objects.
2
Identify the three views and their proponents
From the passage: (1) Permissibility (ibaha) – attributed to al-Shafi'i and some Hanafis like al-Karkhi and supported by al-Hidayah. (2) Prohibition (hurmah) – attributed to Abu Hanifa by others. (3) Suspension (tawaqquf) – chosen in al-Bada'i' by some Hanafis, meaning no ruling is attached until revelation.
The Hanafi school does not have a single position; all three views exist among Hanafis.
3
Analyze the evidence (dalil) for each view
For ibaha: rational benefit – allowing what is not prohibited is easier. For hurmah: caution – avoiding what may be harmful. For tawaqquf: no benefit in ascribing a ruling before revelation, because the ruling is eternal but its attachment to actions only occurs through revelation.
Each view is based on a different understanding of the relationship between reason and revelation.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi disagreement
The passage shows that even among Hanafis there is no consensus. Al-Karkhi and al-Hidayah adopt ibaha; others (attributed to Abu Hanifa) adopt hurmah; al-Bada'i' and others adopt tawaqquf. This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the school.
The mufti must know which view is preferred for fatwa.
5
Determine the mufta bihi (operative) position
According to later Hanafi authorities and al-Hidayah, the relied-upon position is permissibility (ibaha). This is due to ease and because most later scholars favoured it. However, in specific contexts, the mufti may choose another view with proper justification.
The maxim 'al-asl fi al-ashya' al-ibahah' is generally taught as the Hanafi fatwa position.

Source Text

الْأَشْيَاءِ الإِبَاحَةُ عِنْدَ بَعْضِ الْحَنْفِيَّةِ وَمِنْهُمُ الْكَرْخِيُّ وَقَالَ بَعْضُ أَصْحَابِ الْحَدِيْثِ الْأَصْلُ فِيْهَا الْحَظَرُ وَقَالَ بَعْضُ أصْحَابِنَا الأَصْلُ فِيْهَا التَّوَقُفُ بِمَعْنِى أَنَّهُ لَابُدَّ لَهَا مِنْ حُكْمِ لَكِنَّا لَمْ نَقِفْ عَلَيْهِ بِالْعَقْلِ انْتَهى. وَفِى الْهِدَايَةِ مِنْ فَصْلِ الْحِدَادِ أَنَّ الإِبَاحَةَ أَصْلِّ انْتَهى. ترجمه قاعدہ کیا اشیاء میں اصل اباحت ہے یہاں تک کہ عدم اباحت پر دلیل قائم ہو جائے اور امام شافعی کا یہی مسلک ہے۔ یا اشیاء کے اندر حرمت اصل ہے یہاں تک کہ اباحت پر دلیل قائم ہو جائے اور شوافع نے اسے امام ابی حنیفہ کی طرف منسوب تعلق نہیں ہے اس لئے کہ اس کا کوئی فائدہ نہیں ہے، بدائع کا کلام پورا ہوا اور مصنف کی شرح منار میں ہے کہ بعض احناف کے نزدیک اشیاء میں اباحت اصل ہے اور ان ہی میں سے امام کرخی رحمتہ اللہ علیہ ہیں اور بعض اصحاب حدیث کا قول ہے کہ ان (اشیاء) میں اصل ممانعت ہے اور ہمارے بعض احناف کا قول ہے کہ ان اشیاء) میں اصل توقف ہے اس کے معنی یہ ہیں کہ ان کا حکم تو لامحالہ ہے لیکن ہم عقل سے اس پر مطلع نہیں ہوتے اور ہدایہ کی فصل الحداد میں ہے کہ اصل اباحت ہے ہدایہ کی بات پوری ہوئی۔ تشریح : بحث اشیاء میں اصل اباحت ہے یا حرمت؟ اگر کتاب وسنت میں کسی چیز کا حکم نہ ملے تو ایسی چیزوں میں اباحت اصل ہے یا حرمت اس سلسلہ میں تین قول نقل کئے گئے ہیں۔ (۱) اباحت جس کے قائل امام شافعی میں علامہ کرخی اور صاحب ہدایہ کا بھی یہی قول ہے۔ (۲) امام ابوعیف کی طرف اشیاء میں اصل حرمت ہے کو منسوب کیا گیا ہے جبکہ بعض محمد تین بھی اس کے قائل ہیں۔ (۳) صاحب بدائع اور بعض دیگر احناف کہتے ہیں کہ اشیاء میں اصل توقف ہے جب تک شریعت اس پر کوئی علم نہ لگائے اور یہ چیز بدون شرع عقل سے معلوم نہیں ہو سکتی۔
Qa'ida: Is the default in things permissibility until evidence indicates otherwise, and this is the madhhab of al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him; or prohibition until evidence indicates permission, and the Shafi'is attribute this to Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him. In al-Bada'i', the chosen view is that there is no ruling for actions before revelation, and the ruling with us, even if eternal, here means it is not attached to the action before revelation, so the attachment is negated for lack of benefit, end. In Sharh al-Manar by the author, may Allah have mercy on him: the default in things is permissibility according to some Hanafis, among them al-Karkhi; and some ahl al-hadith said the default in them is prohibition; and some of our associates said the default in them is suspension, meaning they must have a ruling but we have not attained it by reason, end. In al-Hidayah, from the chapter of ihdad, that permissibility is the default, end. Translation: ... [Urdu commentary] ...
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage defines a foundational qa'ida in usul al-fiqh: what is the default legal status of things (acts or objects) before any revelation? Three positions are presented: (1) Permissibility (ibaha) – held by Imam al-Shafi'i and some Hanafis like al-Karkhi and the author of al-Hidayah. (2) Prohibition (hurmah) – attributed to Abu Hanifa by the Shafi'is, though some Hanafis also hold this. (3) Suspension (tawaqquf) – the view of the author of al-Bada'i' and other Hanafis, meaning no ruling is ascribed until revelation provides it; the eternal ruling exists but is not attached to actions before revelation due to lack of benefit. The passage clarifies that there is disagreement within the Hanafi school itself on this issue.

Key Terms
الأصْلُ فِي الْأَشْيَاءِ الإِبَاحَةُ (al-asl fi al-ashya' al-ibahah): The default ruling for things is permissibility until evidence shows otherwise.
التَّوَقُفُ (al-tawaqquf): Suspension: no ruling is assigned to actions before revelation; one remains silent until revelation provides a ruling.
الْحَظَرُ (al-hazr): Prohibition: the default ruling for things is prohibition until evidence indicates permissibility.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) All actions are permissible.
C) There is no ruling attached to them.
D) They are recommended.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school presents three views on the default ruling for things before revelation; the mufta bihi is permissibility (ibaha) as supported by al-Hidayah and later scholars.

Maxim: Original Ruling for Things – Ibaha or Tahrim?
Discussion of the maxim on original ruling for undisclosed things

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'al-asl fi al-ashya' to cases where no textual evidence exists and identify which Hanafi position (ibaha, tahrim, or tawaqquf) yields the ruling for fatwa.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school has three views on the original ruling for things; the mufta bihi in mu'amalat is permissibility.

This lesson explores a fundamental legal maxim that shapes how a mufti rules when the primary sources are silent. You will learn not only the three positions held within the Hanafi school and by other schools, but also how to derive the operative ruling for contemporary issues. The passage from Sharh al-Ashbah wa al-Nazair presents the maxim, its sources, and practical applications.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its three formulations
Identify the three possible default rulings for things in the absence of textual evidence: permissibility (ibaha), prohibition (tahrim), or suspension (tawaqquf). Note which scholars hold each view.
The passage mentions al-Shafi'i (ibaha), some Hanafis (tawaqquf/al-Bada'i'), and a minority attribute tahrim to Abu Hanifa.
2
Understand the scope: maskut 'anhu
Recognize that the maxim applies to matters about which the revealed sources are silent (maskut 'anhu). These are cases not explicitly addressed in Quran, hadith, or consensus.
The passage lists examples: ambiguous animals, unknown plants, rivers with unknown ownership, pigeons, and giraffes.
3
Apply the maxim to the furuat
For each example, work through the ruling according to each of the three positions. Note how the same case yields different results depending on which default is chosen.
Example: a problematic animal is halal under ibaha, haram under tahrim, and suspended under tawaqquf.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi diversity
Recognize that the Hanafi school does not have a single position on this maxim. The passage cites al-Karkhi and al-Hidayah for ibaha, al-Bada'i' for tawaqquf, and a minority for tahrim.
This diversity reflects differences in usul methodology and the role of reason vs. revelation.
5
Determine the mufta bihi for fatwa
Using the tarjih mechanism from the passage, conclude that for transactions (mu'amalat), the permissibility view is often relied upon due to custom and necessity, while the suspension view may be preferred in matters of worship ('ibadat).
This step is crucial for a mufti to know which view to adopt when issuing a fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قاعِدَة: هل الأصْلُ فِي الْأَشْيَاءِ الإِبَاحَةُ حَتَّى يَدُلُّ الدَّلِيلُ عَلى عَدَمِ الإِبَاحَةِ وَهُوَ مَذْهَبُ الشَّافِعِي رَحِمَهُ اللهُ أَوِ التَّحْرِيمُ حَتَّى يَدُلُّ الدَّلِيلُ عَلَىٰ إِبَاحَةٍ وَنَسَبَهُ الشَّافِعِيَّةُ إِلَى أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ رَحِمَهُ اللَّهُوَ فِى الْبَدَائِعِ الْمُخْتَارُ أنْ لَّا حُكْمَ لِلْافْعَالِ قَبْلَ الشَّرْعِ وَالْحُكْمُ عِندَنَا وَإِنْ كَانَ أَزَلِيَّا فَالْمُرَادُ بِهِ هُنَا عَدَمُ تَعَلَّقِهِ بِالْفِعْلِ قَبْلَ الشَّرْعِ فَانْتَفَى التَّعَلُقُ لِعَدَمِ فَائِدَتِهِ انْتَهَى وَفِي شَرْحِ الْمَنَارِ لِلْمُصَنِّفِ رَحِمَهُ اللهُ الْأَصْلُ فِي الْأَشْيَاءِ الإِبَاحَةُ عِنْدَ بَعْضِ الْحَنْفِيَّةِ وَمِنْهُمُ الْكَرْخِيُّ وَقَالَ بَعْضُ أَصْحَابِ الْحَدِيْثِ الْأَصْلُ فِيْهَا الْحَظَرُ وَقَالَ بَعْضُ أصْحَابِنَا الأَصْلُ فِيْهَا التَّوَقُفُ بِمَعْنِى أَنَّهُ لَابُدَّ لَهَا مِنْ حُكْمِ لَكِنَّا لَمْ نَقِفْ عَلَيْهِ بِالْعَقْلِ انْتَهى. وَفِى الْهِدَايَةِ مِنْ فَصْلِ الْحِدَادِ أَنَّ الإِبَاحَةَ أَصْلِّ انْتَهى.
وَيَظْهَرُ أَثَرُ هذا الاخْتِلَافِ فِي الْمَسْكُوتِ عَنْهُ وَيَتَخَرَّجُ عَلَيْهَا مَا أَشْكَلَ حَالُهُ فَمِنْهَا الْحَيْوَانُ الْمُشْكِلُ أمْرُهُ وَالنّبَاتُ الْمَجْهُولُ سَمِّيَتُهُ وَمِنْهَا إِذَا لَمْ يُعْرَفْ حَالُ النَّهْرِ هَلْ هُوَ مُبَاحٌ أَوْ مَمْلُوْكَ وَ مِنْهَا لَوْ دَخَلَ بُرْجَةَ حَمَّاهِ وَشَكَ هَلْ هُوَ مُبَاحٌ أَوْ مَمْلُوْكَ وَ مِنْهَا مَسْتَلَةُ الزَّرَافَةِ فَمَذْهَبُ الشَّافِعِيِّ الْقَائِلِ بالإِبَاحَةِ الْحِلٌّ فِي الْكُلِّ وَ أَمَّا مَسْتَلَةً الزِّرَافَةِ فَالْمُخْتَارُ عِنْدَهُمْ حَلُّ أَكْلِهَا وَقَالَ السُّيُوطِيْ وَلَمْ يَذْكُرْهَا أَحَدٌ مِّنَ الْمَالِكِيَّةِ وَالْحَنْفِيَّةِ وَقَوَاعِدُهُمْ تَقْتَضَى حِلَّهَا وَاللَّهُ تَعَالَى أَعْلَمُ.
Maxim: Is the original ruling for things permissibility until evidence indicates lack of permissibility – and this is the school of al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him – or prohibition until evidence indicates permissibility – and the Shafi'is attributed this to Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him. In al-Bada'i', the chosen view is that there is no ruling for actions before revelation; and the ruling with us, even though it is eternal, the intended meaning here is that it does not attach to the action before revelation, so attachment is negated due to lack of benefit. End. In Sharh al-Manar by the author, may Allah have mercy on him: The original ruling for things is permissibility according to some Hanafis, among them al-Karkhi. Some hadith scholars said the original ruling for them is prohibition. Some of our companions said the original ruling for them is suspension, meaning they inevitably have a ruling, but we have not come to know it by reason. End. In al-Hidayah, from the chapter on ihdad, that permissibility is the original. End.
And the effect of this difference appears in what is passed over in silence; and from it are derived cases whose status is ambiguous. Among them: an animal whose matter is problematic, and a plant whose name is unknown; also if the status of a river is unknown – is it permissible (mubah) or owned? Also if a pigeon enters its loft and one doubts whether it is permissible or owned; also the issue of the giraffe. The school of al-Shafi'i, who holds permissibility, says it is permissible in all. As for the issue of the giraffe, the chosen view among them is that eating it is permissible. Al-Suyuti said: No one among the Malikis and Hanafis mentioned it, but their principles imply its permissibility. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage discusses a fundamental legal maxim: what is the original ruling for things when no specific textual evidence exists? The author presents three positions among scholars: (1) The original is permissibility (ibaha) – held by Imam al-Shafi'i, some Hanafis like al-Karkhi, and supported by al-Hidayah. (2) The original is prohibition (tahrim) – attributed to Imam Abu Hanifa by the Shafi'is, though other Hanafis dispute this attribution. (3) The original is suspension (tawaqquf) – the chosen view in al-Bada'i' and some Hanafis, meaning that before revelation, actions have no ruling, and after revelation, the ruling is only known from revelation; reason alone cannot determine it. The author then clarifies an apparent contradiction in the Hanafi position: the ruling is eternal in God's knowledge, but its attachment to actions occurs only after revelation because before revelation there is no benefit in attaching it. The practical effect of this difference appears in cases where the revealed texts are silent (maskut 'anhu). Examples: ambiguous animals, unknown plants, an unowned river, a pigeon entering a loft, and the giraffe. According to the permissibility position, all such cases are permissible. According to the prohibition position, they are prohibited. According to the suspension view, one should refrain from a ruling until evidence is found. The passage also notes that Hanafi and Maliki scholars did not explicitly discuss the giraffe, but their principles (permissibility) would indicate it is permissible to eat.

Key Terms
الأصل في الأشياء (al-asl fi al-ashya): The default ruling for things in the absence of textual evidence
الإباحة (al-ibaha): Permissibility — the ruling that an act is legally neutral, neither rewarded nor punished
التحريم (al-tahrim): Prohibition — the ruling that an act is forbidden
التوقف (al-tawaqquf): Suspension — withholding judgment until evidence is found
المسكوت عنه (al-maskut 'anhu): That about which the revealed sources are silent

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the practical effect (thamar) of the difference on the maxim 'al-asl fi al-ashya'?
A) It only affects matters of worship, not transactions.
B) It appears in cases where the revealed texts are silent (maskut 'anhu), such as ambiguous animals or unknown plants.
C) It has no practical effect because all schools agree on permissibility.
D) It only applies to issues of personal hygiene and cleanliness.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's reliance on permissibility (ibaha) for novel matters in mu'amalat ensures ease, while the suspension view preserves caution where needed.

Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims
intermediate
12 min
Rule: Origin in Private Parts is Prohibition
Stating a maxim and applying it to cases of forgetfulness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'al-asl fi al-abda' al-tahrim' to cases of uncertainty in marital relations and explain why taharri is not permitted in such matters.

Core Idea
In matters of private parts, prohibition prevails until certainty of permissibility is established.

The Hanafi legal maxim 'The origin in private parts is prohibition' (الأصل في الأبضاع التحريم) sets a strict default for all matters involving sexual relations. This is not a rule of absolute prohibition but a presumption that can only be overcome by clear evidence of permissibility, such as a valid marriage. In this lesson, you will see how this maxim governs cases of doubt and prohibits the use of personal judgment (taharri) in these sensitive matters.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its source
First, identify the Arabic maxim: 'al-asl fi al-abda' al-tahrim'. Note its explanation in Kashf al-Asrar: marriage itself was originally prohibited but made permissible due to necessity (darurah).
This establishes the default ruling of prohibition for all matters related to private parts.
2
Understand the exception for necessity
Recognize that marriage is the only exception to this maxim, permitted by divine law due to the necessity of procreation and social order. All other sexual relations remain prohibited unless proven otherwise.
Necessity (darurah) does not extend to cases of doubt; it only allows the original prohibition of marriage to be lifted.
3
Apply the maxim to cases of uncertainty
Examine the examples from al-Kafi: a man who forgets which slave he freed cannot have intercourse with any until the freed one is identified; a man who forgets which wife he divorced with three talaqs cannot approach any until certainty is reached. In these cases, doubt triggers the default prohibition.
Taharri (making a best guess) is explicitly forbidden in such matters because it cannot overcome the default prohibition.
4
Derive the fatwa rule
Conclude that in all matters of furuh (private parts), the default is prohibition. A mufti must require certainty before allowing any permissive ruling. The maxim 'al-yaqin la yazul bi al-shakk' (certainty is not removed by doubt) reinforces this.
This strictness protects lineage and honor from potential violations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَاعِدَة : الأَصْلُ فِي الأَبْضَاعِ التَّحْرِيمُ وَ كَذَا قَالَ فِي كَشْفِ الْأَسْرَارِ شَرْحِ فَخْرِ الإِسْلَامِ الأصْلُ فِى النِّكَاحِ الْحَظَرُ وَابِيْحَ لِلضَّرُورَةِ انْتَهى . فَإِذَا تَقَابَلَ فِي الْمَرْأَةِ حِلٌّ وَ حُرْمَةٌ غُلبَتِ الْحُرْمَةُ وَلِهَذَا لَا يَجُوْزُ التَّحَرِّى فِي الْفُرُوجِ وَفِيْ كَافِي الْحَاكِمِ الشَّهِيدِ مِنْ بَابِ التَّحَرِّى وَلَوْ أَنَّ رَجُلًا لَهُ أَرْبَعُ جَوَارِيَ أَعْتَقَ وَاحِدَةً مِّنْهُنَّ بِعَيْنِهَا ثُمَّ نَسِيَهَا فَلَمْ يَدْرٍ أَيَّتَهُنَّ أعْتَقَ لَمْ يَسَعُهُ أَنْ يَتَحَرَىٰ لِلْوَطِئ وَلَا لِلْبَيْعِ وَلَا يَسَعُ لِلْحَاكِمِ أَنْ يُخَلَّى بَيْنَهُ وَبَيْنَهُنَّ حَتى تَبَيَّنَ الْمُعْتَقَةُ مِنْ غَيْرِهَا وَ كَذَا إِذَا طَلَّقَ إِحْدَى نِسَائِهِ بِعَيْنِهَا ثَلَاثَاً ثُمَّ نَسِيَهَا وَكَذَا لِكَ إِنْ مَيَّزَ كُلَّهُنَّ إِلَّا وَاحِدَةً لَمْ يَسَعْهُ أَنْ يَقْرُبَهَا حَتَّى يَعْلَمَ أَنَّهَا غَيْرُ الْمُطَلَّقَةِ وَكَذَلِكَ يَمْنَعُهُ الْقَاضِي عَنْهَا حَتَّى يُخْبَرَ أَنَّهَا غَيْرُ الْمُطَلَّقَةِ فَإِذَا أَخْبَرَ بِذلِكَ إِسْتَحْلَفَهُ الْبَيَّةَ أَنَّهُ مَا طَلَّقَ هَذِهِ بِعَيْنِهَا ثَلمَّا ثُمَّ حَلَى بَيْنَهُمَا فَإِنْ كَانَ حَلَفَ وَهُوَ جَاهِلٌ بِهَا فَلَا يَنْبَغِي لَهُ أَنْ يَقْرُبَهَا.
ترجمه قاعده فروج میں اصل حرمت ہے اس لئے کشف الاسرار شرح مفخر الاسلام میں فرمایا کہ نکاح میں اصل ممانعت ہے اور ضرورت کی وجہ سے مباح قرار دیا گیا انتہی ۔ لہذا جب عورت میں حلت و حرمت کا تقابل ہو تو حرمت کو تر جیح دی جائے گی اس لئے فروج میں تحری جائز نہیں ہے حاکم شہید کافی کے باب التحری میں ہے کہ اگر کسی کے پاس چار باندیاں ہوں ان میں سے اس نے کسی متعینہ باندی کو آزاد کر دیا پھر اسے بھول گیا اور یاد نہیں رہا کہ اس نے کے آزاد کیا تھا تو اس کے لئے اس کی گنجائش نہیں کہ پٹی یا بیچ کے لئے تحری کرے اور نہ ہی حاکم کے لئے اس کی گنجائش ہے کہ اس کے اور ان (باندیوں ) کے درمیان تخلیہ کرائے یہاں تک کہ آزاد کردہ باندی دوسروں سے متر ہو جائے اور یہی حکم اس صورت میں ہے جب کوئی اپنی بیویوں میں سے کسی ایک کو متعینہ طور پر تین طلاق دیدے پھر اسے بھول جائے اور یہی حکم اس صورت میں ہے جب بجز ایک کے ان تمام میں تمیز کرے تو اس کے لئے یہ گنجائش نہیں ہے کہ اس (ایک) سے صحبت کرے یہاں تک کہ اس کے غیر مطلقہ ہونے کا یقین ہو جائے اور ایسے ہی قاضی اسے اس کے پاس جانے سے رو کے یہاں تک کہ اسے بتا دیا جائے کہ یہ غیر مطلقہ ہے اور جب اسے اس کی خبر دی جائے تو اس سے وجو با حلف لے کہ اس نے اس متعینہ بیوی کو تین طلاق دیں پھر ان دونوں میں تخلیہ کر دے پس اگر اس نے اس سے الاعلمی میں قسم کھائی تو اس کے لئے اس سے صحبت درست نہیں۔
Rule: The origin in private parts (abda') is prohibition. Similarly, it is stated in Kashf al-Asrar, Sharh of Fakhr al-Islam: 'The origin in marriage is prohibition; it was made permissible due to necessity.' End quote. So when permissibility and prohibition confront each other regarding a woman, prohibition prevails. Therefore, taharri (making a best guess) is not permissible in matters of private parts. And in Kafi al-Hakim al-Shahid, in the chapter on taharri: If a man has four slave girls and he frees a specific one of them, then forgets which one, he is not allowed to make taharri for intercourse or for sale, and the judge is not allowed to let him be alone with them until the freed one is distinguished from the others. Similarly, if he divorces one of his wives specifically with three divorces, then forgets which one; similarly, if he distinguishes all except one, he is not allowed to approach her until he knows that she is not the divorced one. Likewise, the judge prevents him from her until he is informed that she is not the divorced one. When he is so informed, the judge administers an oath that he did not divorce this specific one three times, then he allows them to be together. But if he swore while ignorant of her, he should not approach her.
Translation of the rule: The origin in private parts is prohibition. Therefore, in Kashf al-Asrar, Sharh of Fakhr al-Islam, it is stated: 'The origin in marriage is prohibition; it was made permissible due to necessity.' Hence, when permissibility and prohibition confront each other regarding a woman, prohibition is given precedence. For this reason, taharri is not permissible in private parts. In Kafi al-Hakim al-Shahid, in the chapter on taharri: If a person has four slave girls and he frees a specific one of them, then forgets and does not remember which one he freed, then it is not permissible for him to make taharri for intercourse or for sale, nor is it permissible for the judge to let him be alone with them until the freed one is distinguished from the others. The same ruling applies when a person divorces one of his wives specifically with three divorces, then forgets it. And the same ruling applies when he distinguishes all except one: it is not permissible for him to approach her until he is certain that she is not the divorced one. Similarly, the judge prevents him from her until he is informed that she is not the divorced one. When he is informed of that, the judge administers an oath to him that he did not divorce this specific wife three times, then he allows them to be together. But if he swore while he was ignorant of her, then it is not proper for him to approach her.
Source pages p. 318

Commentary
This passage presents a fundamental Hanafi maxim: 'The origin in private parts (abda') is prohibition.' This is a subset of the larger rule that marriage itself is originally prohibited except due to necessity. The practical consequence is that whenever there is a conflict between permissibility and prohibition concerning a woman, prohibition is given preference. Therefore, 'taharri' (making an effort to determine the correct ruling by personal judgment) is not allowed in matters of sexual relations, because of the strictness required. The passage then cites examples from al-Kafi of al-Hakim al-Shahid: a man with four slave girls who freed one but forgot which; he cannot rely on guesswork (taharri) for intercourse or sale, nor can the judge allow him access until the freed slave girl is identified. Similarly, if a man divorces one of his wives with three divorces and forgets which, he cannot approach any wife until certainty is achieved. If he remembers all but one, he cannot approach the remaining one until he knows she is not the divorced one. The judge must prevent him from her until the matter is clear, and if information surfaces that she is not the divorced one, the judge administers an oath for reassurance. This underscores the extreme caution in matters of furuh.

Key Terms
الأبضاع (al-abda'): Private parts; in legal context, it refers to matters of marriage, sexual relations, and reproductive organs.
التحري (al-taharri): Making a best guess or exerting effort to determine the likely ruling in a situation of uncertainty.
الحظر (al-hazar): Prohibition; original legal status of something being disallowed unless evidence lifts it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man has four wives and divorces one of them with three talaqs but forgets which one. According to the Hanafi maxim, what should he do?
A) He may make taharri (best guess) and avoid the one he thinks might be divorced.
B) He must live separately from all wives until the divorced wife is identified with certainty.
C) He may divorce all of them to be safe.
D) He can ask his wives to testify who is divorced and rely on that.

Key Takeaway
The origin in private parts is prohibition; this default can only be overcome by certainty of permissibility, and taharri is never allowed in these matters.
